A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash value of tho Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 23, 1892,
The Duke of Clarence.
One day last week Albert Victor, Duke of
Clarence and Avondale, and lineal heir to
the crown of that empire on which the sun
never sets, succumbed to a complication of
complaints, and departed unexpectedly to
sleep with his fathers. The death of Albert
Victor was practically a foregone conclusion
when he assumed the ill-fated title of
Clarence. An evil fortune has followed
that dukedom from first to last the
Clarences have never done anything in par-
ticular, except die and leave no heirs behind
them. The departure of the last of them
has been attended by all the customary pomp
and riot of dead royalty. A period of mourn-
ing has been proclaimed in Britain. There
is a margin of deep black round the obituary
notices in the papers, and sundry bells have
been rung in a muffled monotone, and
sundry flags have been hoisted half-mast
high in token of lamentation. The London
illustrated papers, which usually devote
themselves exclusively to pictures of the
Prince of Wales laying a foundation-stone,
are hard at work fixing up fanciful sketches
showing horny-handed but mythical toilers
weeping over a loss that can never, by any
possibility, be repaired. And it is under-
stood, in a general and orthodox sort of way,
that the whole English-speaking races are
bathed in woe—and yet, excepting the royal
family, with whose grief we can all sympa-
thise on purely human grounds, the mourn-
ing is unreal enough. The departed Duke
was merely a shadow, and his loss is only
the breakage of a god in a pantheon. No-
body is glad he is dead, except the news-
paper men who have been paid for writing
his obituary ; nobody is particularly sorry ;
everybody is indifferent ; from first to last
there is no active emotion in the matter.
# * *
But, in an emotionless sort of way, The
Bulletin regrets that Clarence has de-
parted. He bade fair to be a great experi-
ment in the art and mystery of monarchy,
and new that experiment is spoilt past all
repair. He was, what he looked—without
exception the most utterly effete, colourless
and inanimate of the Guelphs, and his life
promised to do something towards solving
a question as yet unanswered—how far can a
king approximate to a mere, harmless, unin-
tellectual automaton without injury to the
monarchy ? The two first Georges were
coarse, hard, brutal, ignorant, immoral,
utterly callous, and dull beyond expression,
but they were strong men none the less, with
a species of bull-dog strength which did
them good service in stormy times. The
third was mean, stupid, grasping, partially
deranged, ignorant, and intensely okstinate ;
he was the turning-point of the race,
but in his lucid intervals he was still
a power in the State. After him came
a glittering mountebank and a fool with
an umbrella, and then Victoria showed
the Guelphic race minus much of its
mental energy, but with its physical strength
still unimpaired. Wales is an inferior
edition of his mother, with a certain shallow
shrewdness and a shattered frame ; and
Clarence came at the end of the procession,
with a ruined constitution, a vacant mind,
and a pallid countenance—the representative
on his father’s side of the worn-out dynasty
of England, and on his mother’s of the
effete dynasty of broken Denmark. Pos-
sibly he would have been a successful
monarch simply by reason of these deficien-
cies. In almost any other State he would—
owing to his stupidity—have been the last of
the Guelphs, but in England the first,
second, and third requisites of a sovereign
are that lie must be useless—more useless—
most useless. He must have no ambition,
no energy, no longing for action in any
shape, for these things all make a monarch
desire to do something, and the great pro-
viso of the British Constitution nowadays is
that he must do nothing—absolutely no-
thing. And Clarence was, in many re-
spects, so absolutely fitted to be an ideal
British sovereign that The Bulletin regrets
his loss. England will find it hard to raise
so nobly inanimate a person again this
generation.
# * #
The Guelphs, in these latter days, are
beginning to taper off towards extinction.
George of Wales, the new heir to the
Imperial crown, is an individual with a repu-
tation for physical vigour which is partly real
and partly owing to the fictitious portraits
published in the illustrated papers, but even
he is only the shadow of his brawny, hoarse,
red, hard-swearing, hard-drinking fore-
fathers—those old kings who kept a harem
of gorgons apiece, and ate every day as if
there was no hereafter. By British law he
is allowed to marry no one save a princess of
royal descent, and long inter-marriage among
the dreadfully unwholesome royalties of
Europe has reduced them all to one common
level of disease and weak-mindedness. There
has been a great want of healthy and muscu-
lar usurpers of recent years—of men who had
strength and vigour enough to snatch at a
crown and to hold it afterwards with a firm
grip against all comers—and for lack of them
the close corporation of monarchs is fast
becoming a corporation of imbeciles. If the
dynasty is to be preserved, it would be better
for the Guelphs that George of Wales
should marry a plain cook, with brawny
limbs and the blood of a long line of cart-
horses in her veins—better for the monarchy
even that, like Marshal Bazaine, he should
pick up his consort at night beneath a
lamp-post—than that he should bring a
princess of one of the old reigning
houses of Europe to the English throne.
The blood of Bernadotte, the private
soldier of fortune, lent a little freshnessto the
stagnant stream, but three generations of
intermarriage with contemporary dynasties
has reduced the Swedish house to the
customary level. The fierce courage and
tireless energy of the old pig-jobber who
snatched the Servian crown is gone, and his
descendents are incurable mediocrities; it
takes a wonderfully short time for a pig-
dealer’s sons to degenerate in the atmos-
phere of royalty. Therefore, George of
Wales, the effete son of a worn-out race, will
doubtless marry the effete daughter of some
other race equally decayed, and the Guelphs
will keep on going bad in public for the joy
and edification of the empire. All the
dynasties of Europe are going bad, and so
much public putrefaction is a cheerless
spectacle.
• * #
A tendency to crumble away suddenly is
one of the growing characteristics of the
Guelphs in recent years. They break up
unaccountably, like aged opium-eaters or
men who have lived on arsenic. The Prince
of Wales promises to die of old age while
his mother is still comparatively young, just
as his own son died—practically of old age—
at 28, while his father was still alive and
still capable of laying foundation-stones with
at least a remnant of his former vigour.
Albert Victor, even at his best, had hardly
the capacity to lay a foundation-stone ; he
was the emblem of a generation that was
born aged, and was too weak and nerveless
for any amusement that is fit for public
mention. If he had lived, however, he
would probably have been the father of a
line of kings, and the end of the
Guelphs could then have been foretold with
reasonable accuracy. Some day in the dim
future the last and feeblest of a line of
crowned imbeciles would have been for-
gotten and abandoned in his palace, even as
the deranged Charles VI. was in the halls
of the French kings, when the Duke of
Burgundy went prowling at night through
the huge, deserted castle in search of a
potentate who had been mislaid by his loving
subjects, and found the lost monarch half-
naked and hungry, crouching and gibbering
over a dying fire, and drivelling abjectly
among the ashes. Or else, it may be, the
last of the Guelphs would have been a re-
production* of the last of the Valois —that
monarch who dressed himself in female
attire, and spent his days at a spinning-
wheel, and wandered about his capital in a
carriage with his arms full of small dogs and
pet monkeys. In either case the British
nation is so loyal, or else so indifferent, that
it would hardly depose even a Guelph. The
last king of the dynasty will probably stray
away in the final stages of Ins mental decay,
and the monarchy will become extinct
through failure to recover him, and the
republic, instead of being established by any
fixed purpose, will come by accident. And
hero, again, The Bulletin wastes a vain
regret over Albert Victor’s demise. He
was so eminently fitted to leave behind him
a royal family like that famous House of
V Alois, which dragged itself to death through
200 years of wretchedness and howling in-
sanity -so admirably suited to rush the
royal line of the Guelphs through idiocy to
prompt extinction—that the unborn republic
sheds a tear over his dust and feels that it
has lost a friend.
* # #
It is an eloquent example of the irony of
fate, that only a few days ago the Parliament
of the Cape Colony voted a large sura of
money as a present to the dead prince, on
the occasion of that wedding which is now
postponed to all eternity. So far as we
know, the Cape Colony—under the guidance
of that famous “empire-builder,” Mr. Cecil
Rhodes —was the only one among the many
dependencies of Britain which took sufficient
interest in Clarence’s existence to waste its
revenue in his honour. Ten years ago, every
province of Australasia would have sent its
official contribution out of the public funds
and its outburst of grovel on such an occa-
sion ; now, no Australian politician would
dare to submit to Parliament a proposal that
such a thing should be done. But then, Aus-
tralia is new, and England is very, very old
and stereotyped. Therefore it is difficult to
foretell events, but the chances seem to be
that Albert Victor’s death is a great gain
to the British monarchy. The end of the
monarchical system and the beginning of the
republic will come, in all probability, only
when the last Guelph becomes so deranged
as to necessitate his being chained to his
throne, while he gives his royal assent to
Parliamentary enactments in a strait-jacket.
The deceased Clarence promised to accelerate
this consummation, and his loss has post-
poned it—for a time. But the result will
come all the same. The imbecility which
troubles all the royal houses of Europe is in
the Guelphic blood, and with every succes-
sive generation the disease makes further
progress.
The Quacks of an Ancient Race.
Great is Diana of the Ephesians, and England,
the modern Diana of that ilk, is so overgrown
with the magnitude of her stupendousness that
earth lacks atmosphere enough to carry off the
superfluous vapour from the fumes of her
national swelled head. Mr. W. T. Stead the
other day enlarged somewhat in a vigorously
glorified, inconsequential and illogical fashion
concerning the almightiness of the almighty
British empire; and he likewise published
many columns concerning three colonial builders
of that ungainly edifice—two of whom have
since died, the one in the flesh and the other in
the spirit—and now a person named Browne,
with an “e,” and a hyphenated prefix spelt
Crichton, and a vanguard of titular obloquy
figuring as Sir James, who is, moreover,
alleged to be an eminent physician, rushes with
gay alacrity to the forefront of the fight to save
the glory of the English-speaking race. He is
mortally injured in several parts of his
emotional anatomy by a consideration of the
wholesome fact that the expansion of Australian
universities has a distinct and healthy tendency
to lessen the number of students sent to
Oxford and Cambridge and other similar em-
poriums of concentrated essence of antique
prejudice. He regards—and herein is expressed
the Imperial Jingoistic faddism of the man
named Browne, with an “ e -he regards this
education of “colonial” youths at English
universities as important, inasmuch as students
would return to Australia filled with a love efi
England and of English institutions.
* # *
There has, within the past few years, grown
up an English-speaking - union cult which
vapours considerably about a connection under
one rule of the peoples who have their origin in
an Anglo-Saxon dictionary, but whose various
blood-strains are about as diverse as those of
the many races that go to make up the
Austrian Empire. Such a union is somewhat
of substance less than the pallid adumbration
of the shadow of a dream; for not only will
America and England never coalesce, but the
widening breach between England and her
colonies will continue to grow until it is
absolutely unbridgeable. And this will follow
simply because the question of sentiment,
which Imperial Federationists lay so much
stress upon, is not in the least involved. We
can never lose our part in the alleged
glories of the race, even though we were
separated from England ten thousand times ;
nay, even though the British Isles were taken
up bodily and flung into space or sunk beneath
the sea. Shakespeare and , Bacon, Alfred
the Great and Magna Charta will be as much
our property when we are a confederacy of
Republican States as they are at present, or as
they ever were. Literature itself belongs to
whoever can read and understand it, and in
this sense Lowell or Whittier belongs to us
quite as much as does Marcus Clarke or
Henry Kendall. But there is this difference—
Lowell and Bret Haute are distinctly Ameri-
cans. using the English language. The senti-
ment, the local colour, the local usages of
speech belong to their own country. The mere
fact of a common dictionary does not make the
literatures of different countries identical. If
one only cares to go back far enough, France,
Spain, Portugal, and Italy derive their speech
from a common source. They are, moreover,
designated the Latin races, when, as everybody
knows, they are made up of Kelts, Teutons,
Goths, Vandals, Huns—and many otherspeciesof
savages who variously and barbarously adopted
the polite tongue of the outworn Roman
gentlemen they overthrew. A common dic-
tionary as a basis of political union is about as
logical a proposition as a common interest in a
graveyard, a common possession of red heads
or wooden legs, or a common propensity to
colonial beer or ice-cream. In the Review op
Reviews, the other day, a Mr. Astley Cooper
proposed a scheme of Imperial Federation based
on cricket, boat-racing, and sprint-running ! ! '
* # *
Christie Murray tells a story of a young
Australian who was taken to see Westminster
Abbey, but who stoutly maintained the supe-
riority in architectural grandeur of the Ballarat
Town Hall. This is decidedly uncultured, but
it is highly gratifying to Australians, and
Christie Murray, as a consistent Englishman,
must admire, although he may pretend to sneer
at it. It is the sentiment that his countrymen
carry about with them from London to Paris,
to Vienna, to Rome, to Khartoum, to India, to
Hades. It is the sentiment which, when
spoken of by Englanders of Englanders, is
called patriotism ; when applied to “ Kaw-
lonials,” “ blasted impertinence.” For all that,
it has made Christie Murray’s country one of
the most hated by other nations of any under
the sun, and it is, moreover, one of the things
we do not want to learn from the great mother
and mistress ,of Podsnappery. As being indi-
cative of a wholesome distrust of the influence
of Gothic architecture on the unsophisticated
Australian intellect, we rapturously commend
the remark of the young man from Ballarat, for
herein lies a deeper philosophy than meets the
eye.
* * *
__ There is nothing so enduring as architecture.
The pyramids are Egypt, Athens is the Acro-
polis, ancient Rome lingers yet in the ruins of
the Forum. The Pope will reign as long as one
stone stands upon another in the stately edifice
dedicated to St. Peter. England’s Queen is a
section of Westminster Abbey. Think of the
character of Charles I.—was it not Oxford
University ? The boasted Constitution of
England is pure Gothic architecture introduced
into legislation. This is the secret of the in-
fluence of the English University. It belongs
to the recorded order of things. It is a memo-
rial of precedent upon precedent. Its quad-
rangles shelter Conservatism, and its courts
are bound in the spirit with the musty memories
of the past. Oxford is synonymous with narrow-
ness, with Toryism, with a frantic clutching at
the mildewed traditions of the Middle Ages,
just as London University is synonymous with
all that fosters what is broad, liberal, progres-
sive and scientific. Through the influence of
its splendid architecture Oxford dwarfs the
human intellect that lives beneath the shadow
of its venerable walls, and the man that there
sucks in his mental nutriment is bound hence-
forth and for ever to the continuance of the
order of things that be. He for all time is a
true and trusty adherent of Church and State
and Crown, and this is the reason why Sir
James Browne, with an “e,” desires our youth
to surrender themselves to the seductive in-
fluence of such hallowed homes of learning as
Oxford and Cambridge.
* * #
We love not England ! It is not for us ! For
this reason must we sternly refuse all modes of
seductive endearment. England belongs to the
venerable and moss-grown past—the past of
gray—walls of Gothic cathedrals, of kings and
tournaments, of knights and the burning of
many witches. England is a broad, expansive
moss-trooper with a policy of Freetrade and a
bludgeon of mediaeval sacerdotalism. In the
wake of the trader treads the bishop, about
whose vestments cling memories of Oxford and
Archbishop Laud and the Star Chamber and
many other iniquities. We are young and
coltish. We cannot afford Freetrade because
we have no standing army. Our weapon is not
a bayonet and Bible-supported rum-bottle, but
the axe of the pioneer. We do not favour
university education very much either, because
we are compelled to put our sons to some sort
of use, and a too close perusal of the ecclesiasti-
cal tombstones of the past lias a tendency to
unfit a man for the genuine work of a Repub-
lican and democratic existence. And, finally,
we do not want to be tied up to England
because she uses the same spelling-book. We
would rather spell “colour” without a “u,”
and go over to the United States as full-fledged
Yankees.
The Apotheosis of a Gaol Flogger.
By appointing the public flagellator, oneELLORY,
to the responsible and respectable position of
Governor of Deniliquin Gaol, the New South
Wales Government has administered to an
already sufficiently-detestable service a hypo-
dermic injection of the virus of the old Convict
System, under which the champion expert in
callous brutality was rapidly preferred to the
highest promotion. Clean, free minds will hold
no excuse valid for Minister O’Connor’s almost
unaccountable action, and for his quite unac-
countable defence of it. Parallel with this
hideous aberration, Mr. O’Connor has emerged
into official light as a minor Howard (not
“ Nosey Bob”) of prison reform, by being the
first New South Wales Minister for Justice to
issue to prisoners licenses-of-liberation, or
tickets-of-leave, under permissive provisions of
the Criminal Law' Amendment Act of 1883 ; and
that, too, without waiting for the slow red-
tapeism of the completion of a code of regula-
tions now in process of being drafted •. To
such a Minister of Justice, hopeful persons
looked for such reforms as the Abolition
of the Lash, and the Abolition of Capital
Punishment ; yet this same Minister abases
himself to do high honour to the odious office
of Public Flagellator —half-brother to that
of Public Executioner. Further, he apparently
glories in this Obi-worship, in a minute in which
he refuses to, reverse his action (italics ours)
Of the officers qualified fo fill this post, Mr. F.Ii.ORV
(think of the flower being a Mister!), by reason of his
service ani grade, stood first entitled to promotion. He
has a total service of over 17 years, and is a senior warder
of eight years’ standing.

prisoners, a departmental ukase was issued,

# ?
The cancellation of Scourger Ellory’s
appointment must be insisted on, and such
insistence persisted in, both inside and outside
of Parliament. This country cannot afford to
re-inoculate itself with the cancerous lymph ot
a Btamped-out leprosy. So long as we continue
in the brutal folly of breaking necks and flaying
backs, in the desecrated name of Justice, at
least let Common Hangmen and Common
Scourgers remain as they have elected to
become. If the reason for Scourger Ellory s
sudden glorification is to be found in acts ot
past personal flunkeyism performed to any high
politician, then the latter and his friend the
Scourger should both be brought down together,
if the Scourger cannot be isolated from his
influential patron. Again, is not that influen-
tial patron afraid that some malice-nursing
prisoner may take the ex-Scourger’s life Is it
lobe left to modern convictism to wipe out this
reappearing stain of the old convictism ?

“We.”

The Elder Exploring Expedition.
The last telegram received from the Elder
“ exploring ” party, now threading their dan-
gerous way through outside stations, goldfields
and telegraph-stations, would seem to intimate
that the strike epidemic has reached even them.
The doctor, the geologist, the botanist, and
another man, have resigned in a body. As the
second in command resigned some short time
back, and another left at Esperance Bay, the
party will soon be in workable order, especially
by the t ime they kill a few more camels. For
pure humour, this so-termed exploring expedi-
tion will rank as the funniest in the annals of
our history. It is under the control of the
“Royal Geographical Society of Australasia,'
presumably so-called because most of the mem-
bers know nothing whatever about the geography
of Australia. They concocted a set of rules for
the guidance of their “officers,” and a map was
prepared whereon the country to be explored
was marked down, quite regardless of the fact
that a large portion of it was under settlement
and a good deal of the rest perfectly well-
known. The leader is noted as a smart man on
the Stock Exchange! and the members of
that institution gave him a dinner before
his departure. Since then the telegrams
that have come in have been a monotonous
record of visits to various well-known spots
and centres of settlement in Western Aus-
tralia. But the joke lies in the fact that the
people interested still persist in regarding it as
an exploring party. The leader sends and
receives telegrams and despatches. One of the
members of "the G.S. read a paper, in Hobart,
before the pseudo-scientific association, on the
doings of this wonderful expedition, and, to put
a climax on it all, the London Times —poor
Times I—had an article on some sensational
cablegram that had been sent to England,
bemoaning “ the future of Central Australia in
consequence of Lindsay’s unfavourable report.”
Wanted—A Crown Defender.
Ever since Magna Charta announced that
justice should not be sold, the richest criminals
have always hired the best lawyers, while poor
prisoners have had to plead guilty or else
blunder along to almost certain conviction.
The rich man can generally obtain an alibi and
an oration with enough points to crowd a
cattle-truck reserved for the Full Court; the
undefended prisoner may have bis interest
guarded by a Judge, who can do no more than
guess at the defence. Conviction depends in a
large measure upon the worth of a prisoner’s
cheque-book, and although poverty per se is no
crime, it commonly leads to conviction therefor.
These statements are not entirely novel; we
have heard something like them before on the
stage and stump and elsewhere, but we use
them for the purpose of indicating a plan of
improving the administration of justice. Our
present idea is that, until something better is
suggested, all accused persons should be
defended by a Crown Advocate employed to
defend, just as the Crown Prosecutors are now
employed to prosecute, and that no other advo-
cates should be allowed to appear for the
defence. This plan seems to be a little revolu-
tionary and otherwise objectionable until you
get used to it, but it has a lot of advantages
about it, and it has a recommendation by
analogy in its favour. Every reason in favour
of prosecution at State expense and by a State
official is at least equally strong as applied to
criminal defence. In England at the present
day only a certain number of crimes of strictly
public concern are prosecuted by the State ; in
other cases the prosecutor has to look after the
case himself and hire a lawyer to keep it going
for him. Here are the faults of such a system,
and the power it gave to the owner of
much coin to buy “justice ” in large quantities
induced the adoption of the method now in
vogue. In Australia the private prosecutor
may not even hire a barrister to “ assist
the Crown ; however anxious the prose-
cutor may be to get a conviction he is not
allowed to use his own coin for that purpose.
All cases are prosecuted under this regime with
an even quantum of skill, but defences are
“ prepared” and conducted with the very
highest skill and care, or else not conducted at
all, according to the financial circumstances of
the accused. But to the latter part of the rule
there is one exception. Years ago it dawned in
a vague kind of way upon the Judges that it
was really not quite fair to a man who was
being tried for his life, to be left to his
own” ignorant helplessness in meeting, the
charge preferred against him, and the cus-
tom then obtained of “assigning” the
youngest barrister who happened to be
loafing in Court, to defend the prisoner.
Generally the prisoner was not much advant-
aged by this assistance, but the expedient gave
an appearance of favour to the accused.
Recently in N.S. Wales the practice was
extended one long step further. Now, if any
person accused of a capital offence is without
means to employ counsel, a solicitor and a
barrister are retained by the Crown and paid
to undertake the defence. Parenthetically it
may be noted that since the inception of the
rule referred to, hardly any prisoners arraigned
on capital charges have had any money for
their defence: their sudden and almost universal
poverty dates from the change in the practice.
The rule is good as far as it goes, but logically
its extension to cases not capital is necessary.
If it is unfair to a man on trial for his life to
leave him without legal assistance, it is unfair
to try a man on a charge involving penal
servitude for life at similar disadvantage.
If a man accused of murder has a claim on the
Treasury for means of defence, why should
another nian indicted for cattlestealing be re-
fused aid ! The distinction is illogical. Am],
on the other hand, as prosecutions are a public
charge, why should not defence be so ? Is the
public more interested in proving one side than
the other of a criminal question? The Crown
pays a Judge to see fair play to both sales, and
then pays a Crown Prosecutor to prove one side
of the case to the Judge, while the other side
of the case is left wholly to the arbitrament of
the almighty dollar or the absence thereof.
Crimes are of public concern. It is of public
consequence that the guilty should be convicted
and the innocent be acquitted, and these results
will never bo reached until all persons are
defended as they are now prosecuted, by a
public official, and with fairly level professional
skill. The Crown Defender should be the next
addition to the Public Service.
♦
The V.R.C. Committee recently sent a circular
to every registered trainer, jockey and book-
maker in the province warning them that their
licences may be revoked if they maintain any
professional connection with unregistered
meetings. The rapid increase in the ranks, of
racing loafers around Melbourne quite justifies
this autocratic legislation. In every big city
there are hundreds of men ready to throw up.
work and degenerate into “followers of the
turf,” provided that the supply of petty meet-
ings is sufficiently continuous to keep the
infatuation alive. The more racing, the more
followers or hangers-on there will be to give a
seedy, raffish, blasphemous taint to those big
turf shows which have hitherto been accepted
by the public at large as excuses for fitful
bursts of decent dissipation. For business
reasons, pure and simple, the V.R.C. and
kindred clubs are bound to aim at preventing
their solid patrons from being scared off by the
discovery that the social standard of race-goers
is getting lower. And if the inevitable
tendency of unregistered meetings is to
create miserable adventurers and foul-mouthed
vagabonds (who, as they swarm in and
out of race-trains, serve the moralist as shock-
ing examples of what betting brings us to) they
ought to be wiped out, anyway. Also a number
of small registered meetings, where robbery is
as rife as wrangling, and blood and hair fly
about at the slightest provocation, might very
well be wiped out at the same time. 'The most
desirable class of race-meeting is that which
attains to the dignity of a social function, and
where the impetuous bank-clerk is not quite
out of the influence of his “people.” More-
over, a little bit of such function every
now and then is as much as anybody
requires. It is the cant of sporting papers
that the demand creates the supply. As a
matter of fact, supply acts as an evil stimulant
to demand. One State lottery every year
would not enervate the industry of a country,
nor would the annual setting up of a licensed
gaming-table for a couple of days turn Aus-
tralia into a Monaco. But the too constant
presence of inducements to bet on horse-racing
creates a betting habit, and transforms a cer-
tain number of what might be harmless sup-
porters into hangers-on, who ultimately drag
their “ sport ” down into the gutter.

Mr. W. Dudley Flinn, of the Victoitan
Democratic Club, writes thus :
Dear Bulletin, —Your comments in current issue con-
cerning H. J. Wbixon do him great injustice. He is a
veteran member of the Legislature and held office as
Attorney-General as far back as 1870. His personality
has secured him a unique place in our politics, and he is
certainly a square man above toadyism, and the alleged
“ honour ” recently conferred on him has probably come
unsolicited. Anyhow, he never made professions of
extreme radicalism, nor proclaimed his contempt for
titles in the scathing terms attributed to Robert Stout
True, he has .been associated with politicians who
are not in the odour of sanctity with Radicals,
and while contaminated by contact with them he
officially fathered the reactionary measure which you
rightly reprobate, but politicians, like travellers, are oft
times discovered in suspicious company. Nevertheless,
lie has rendered the State some excellent service. His
energetic action in the highly important An Toy appeal
case which he really conducted before the Judicial Com-
mittee of the Privy Council with marked ability, and
which resulted in a reversal of the judgment of our
Supreme Court, and a decision from the highest Court of
Appeal in the empire which established the legality of
legislation by self-governing colonies restricting or ex-
cluding people of alien race, even horn subjects of the
Crown, from such colonies, merits generous acknowledge-
ment from Australasian Radicals. For this service
he received a most hearty and unanimous vote
of thanks from the present Assembly. More-
over, last session he introduced a Bill which abolished
the odious 6 George IV. Act relating to conspiracy in
industrial disputes, and also repealed that portion of . the
Masters and Servants Act which gave exploiters of labour
unjust powers. This Act, which H. J. Wbixon introduced
in an admirable speech, brings Victoria into line with the
latest labour legislation in Great Britain, and is
undoubtedly the most valuable achievement of this Par-
liament from a democratic standpoint, and it is safe to
assert could never have been passed into law by any other
member in the present Parliament. His valuable work in
connection with the Mallee Select Committee.deserves
mention. Sir Henry J. Wbixon has consistently
supported the abolition of plural voting, and
has always strenuously asserted the supremacy of
the Assembly-when conflicts have arisen, or threatened
to arise, betwixt the popular and the unpopu-
lar Houses. He is a most respected member of our
Parliament, whose knowledge, assiduous attention to his
duties, arid dispassionate treatment of vexed questions,
renders him invaluable in debate, and his withdrawal
from Parliament would he a national loss, which, though
we favour antagonistic schools of political and economic
thought, would be deeply regretted.

From Sydney Telegraph’s daily leader on the
beauties of Foreign-trade :
Nothing can be more obvious than that a law which is
intended to specially serve one interest or section of the
community can onlv do so at the expense of the others.
As Mr. Cotton stated the case, a community left without
interference to the exercise of its activities will employ
them to the best purpose.
This is a very large order, and a very insane
one, but of late the Telegraph has taken to
throwing comprehensive generalities off' its
chest in an absolutely reckless manner. Ninety-
nine out of every hundred laws are intended to
“ specially serve one interest or section, of the
community,” for nobody can draft one hill
large enough to serve everybody. The Early-
Closing Bill which the D.T. booms is intended
to serve the shop-assistants only. The
Sydney Electric - lighting Company’s Act
which 'it screams for so persistently is for one
section of the nation only, unless the D.T.
can prove that the light will ho visible at
Bonrke or Wilcannia. The Single-tax prin-
ciple, which it smacks in a fatherly way on the
back, is only for one section —the absolutely
landless class, and the One-man-one-vote system
only serves the class which hasn’t got plural
votes. Every thing the Telegraph ever howled
for or ever will howl for is open to the same
objection, and by its own showing it should
block the Broken Hill Water Supply Bill,
because that measure is only drafted in the
interests .if one town, and the Irrigation pro-
posals because t hey only do good to certain dry
districts, wherefore both of them must be
injurious to the country at large.
The amount of gold extracted from five of
the partially-cleveloped tnfnes at Yilgarn (W.A.)
during 1891 was 15,000 ounces.
The disappearance of the kangaroo from
the more settled districts of West Australia has
been the cause of a motion in the W.A. Assembly
instituting a close season for that animal for a
period of four years. A compromise was agreed
to, protecting the kangaroo from October to April
each year.

published Henry Lucy’s narrative of how the
Princess Teek came to be called “ Cousin
May,” its London cableinan suddenly commenced
to call her the “Princess May,” although he- had,
right up to the previous day, known her only as
“ Princess Victoria.’’
The severity of the drought in the North
West portions of Western Australia is causing
great loss of stock to pastoralists, and it is not
improbable that the'W.A. Government will have
to forego the current year's rents from the
squatters. Scab is also raging with virulence
among the flocks of the Northern stations.

Dear Bulletin, : —ln a recent number 1
saw quoted a paragraph (from an article in a
London magazine) concerning a certain horrible
growth in the luxurious looks of a certain empress.
You say, or rather the article referred to says,
that such a disease is unknown to medical men.
Perhaps to English medical men, but not at all
unknown to medical men on the Continent. I
have seen sufferers from this disease with my own
eyes. It is called, in the vernacular of the
country, “ Welchselkopf.” The Weichsel is a
large river, on the banks of which this malady is
most prevalent. It is perfectly true that this
horrible growth has a nervous system of its own,
as the miserable sufferers know to their cost. I
could tell a horrible story about it, Which I know
to be perfectly true, but out of consideration to
your readers, who have only one nervous system
apiece, I forbear.—Yours truly, Rattle Sang,
Freemantle, W.A., 6th Jan., 1892. [You were
never bom to run a newspaper. Send along the-
horrible story at once—instantly—-full speed. Ed.
Bulletin.]
Dear Bulletin,— Thompson, of Sudds
and Thompson notoriety, though “ disgraced and
degraded ” by Governor Darling,” was not “done
to death ” by that excellent old gentleman. Sudds
was ; Thompson survived some years, and I will
give to the world the particulars of his later life
some day. And, by the way, I may as well
mention that Sudds’ name was not Sudds. It was
Studds, and as such it appears in one, at least,
newspaper report of the time. My informant was
in court the day the two soldiers were charged
with larceny—the offence which led to the subse-
quent infamous proceedings—and had a distinct
recollection of hearing Studds spell his name in
correction of the magistrate’s clerk. But a
common private had no more light to his name
than he had to his soul, at that period, and,the
Court called him Sudds, and Sudds he will
remain, for that is the appellation.burnt indelibly
into the Australian conscience.— Price Warung.
It is related that in the early days of St.
Paul’s College, Sydney, students were few and far
between, and at one time there were only two on
the books, Ranny Want and Kees Jones, now of
Rockhampton. Some benevolent old gentleman
had endowed two scholarships in Divinity, and of
course these young alumni were entered for them.
The day of examination arrived, and they duly
appeared, gowned and capped, hut by no means
eager for the fray ; for, to say the least, neither
had been reared"in an atmosphere of piety. Inc
of the posers put to them was “Give a short
sketch of the life and adventures of St. Paul, with
references.” Ranny, who had been cramming
“Butler’s Analogy ” and kindred works, seems to
have had some cloudy notion of the subject, but
poor Rets was utterly flabbergasted owing to his
total ignorance of anything, connected with the
history of the apostle. When he had to tackle
the paper he turned to his neighbour and said ;
“Who the deuce was St. Paul, Ran., old man?”
They both took prizes, and Ranny was first.

Benzoin It is only those who have seen the wild way in

Perhaps the most, noteworthy series of
contributions to English journalism since Russell’s
Crimean letters has just been concluded in the
letters of “ Veins ” to the Times on the Army and
War-office administration. They have been
attributed to General Ohesney, and ho was called
upon by Broderick, Under-Secretary for War, to
“ exculpate ” himself from the suspicion of having
written them. Ohesney, however, repudiates the
authorship, while bearing testimony to the
remarkabihty of the letters ; and the few words in
which he trounces Broderick spit that upper-crust
Bumble as fatally as a naturalist’s pin does a
moth. “ Vetus’ ” conclusion—editorially adopted
by the Times— is that the Army system of Great
Rritain is rotten to the core; and that the War-
Office is a model of vicious bureaucracy. The
Bulletin would not be surprised to find that
Colonel Maurice turns out to be “ Vetus.” Who
ever the writer, he must bo a man of extraordinary
courage to attack the “ruling classes” in their
closest preserve.-

846 Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, January 20, 1892.
My Dear Moorabinua,—
If Royal etiquette reared its hydra-head in Australian
“court circles” as in England, Lady Jersey’s Maori-
land sprint would be put off until next summer, and I am
prepared to prove that she’s not a social economist if she
doesn’t postpone it, anyhow. Every excuse should be
seized on by the Gov. and his consort which leads to a
solemn withdrawal into their moated grange with a “no
admission” notice hanging over the portcullis ; the full
period of seclusion for the demise of the Prince ought to
be observed to the semi-second by the Jerseys, who could
thus tide over the summer very comfortably in complete
immunity from tennis-parties and tea-parties, with no
grunts and wails about their inhospitality wafting from
the fashionable recuperating districts either. And next
summer—which may present no royal excuse for squelch-
ing the social aspirations of the living embodiments of
the visiting-list—next summer when ruralising sassiety
will expect to be stoked with tea and strawberries on the
paddockish vice-regal lawns at Sutton Forest, Lady
Jersey should beat a strategic retreat to the Land of
Kauri and so give her natural enemies the slip two times
instead of onoe. But if M. E. Jersey isn’t the opportunist
suggested above, she will have fled N.S.W. for Maori-
land, accompanied by her brother, Captain Leigh, and
Lady Galloway ere these logical hints blush in black and
white, and Jersey will be stranded a lone, lorn grass-
widower.
Apropos of our leading lady’s outing, she isn’t going to
let Maoriland off so easily’ when she dawns on that windy
nation, for a Kodak camera (the portable little affair
Baker and Rouse, its commercial god-fathers and god-
mothers, are so pardonably proud of) goes with her to fix
scenic morceaux all along the line of trip. Lady
Jersey, judged by specimens of her handicraft, couldn’t
keep out of the insolvency-court as a practical photo-
grapher, but her amateur efforts, aided by a good
camera, encourage her to go ahead. Quite a harvest of
Kodaks resulted from the recent vice-regal South-western
flight.
A London journal eulogises the Jerseys in a recent
issue, stroking their fur the right way, principally because
of the dexterous manner in which they balance on a
social rail. “Both,” purrs their distant admirer, “have
made themselves equally acceptable to the local Radicals
and Tories, while Lord Jersey has proved himself the
best Governor N.S.W. has ever had.” Now, “George”
—in the short space of 12 introductory months (it is
exactly a year since he arrived)—has lavished his gracious
patronage on every entertainment, short of a dog-fight,
that began with a bar of the national anathema, and
has shed the light of his whiskers on more public-school
(iz-gigs, bazaars, banquets, foundation-stones, fire-engines
and bun-struggles than I could tick off if 1 had a whole
newspaper to do it in—but he isn’t the darling of the
populace, and neither is his literary sposa. Coming after
the free-and-easy Carington regime, the Jerseys’ action in
tliscountenancing, for one thing, mob receptions, has
frost-bitten the cupboard-love of three-quarters of their
lady “subjects.” The fickle Government House females
could have worshipped Lady J. in as hysterical a manner
as they adored Lady C. if Lady J. had been prepared to
suffer their fulsome homage on fixed afternoons and
nourish it with free victuals, but she wasn’t, and there-
fore the female population of N.S.W., taking it “by and
large,” does not exactly idolise her. It isn’t the Aus-
tralian fashion to worship before the altar of an unknown
goddess.
Awstralian tippy-tops have been hugely depressed ever
since the cabled news of the demise of the Royal bride-
groom-elect appeared in big black letters in the papers,
and favoured damsels who, during his Australian visit,
were beckoned out to dance with the deceased Prince
(who always left them to find their own way to their
chaperones when the dance was over—after the manner of
Royalty), and girls who managed a stilted court-etiquette
sort of frozen flirt with the pale and solemn princeling on
his visit to our wilds 11 years ago, do now assume a soft
and pensive melancholy—as of one whose pet canary has
recently suicided in its water-supply. And they have
adopted weeds of ceremonious sorrow, too, but alas!
Court mourning as comprehended in Australia by the
suburban lady whose earthly paradise lies behind the
sentr}' at the gates of Government House, is a pie-
bald and ludicrous outbreak of loyalty. When-
ever a Royalty dies, the female Tory, who is
ever in our midst, rakes among her black-haired
belongings for a pair of gloves, a pair of hosen
and wide crgpe arm-band and an utterly funereal-death-
and-cypress-looking-hat—all of which she pits against the
saddest coloured garment in her wardrobe—and her
sartorial sorrow is complete. The inside of a “fashion-
able” church the first Sunday after the exalted demise is
the place to see the eccentricity of local “ Court mourn-
ing.” Black badges on frocks of every colour; hats
trimmed like flower-pots with red roses, blue forget-me-
nots (do you say that “ blue forget-me-nots” is superfluous
detail? I was shown artificial pink forget-me-nots the
other day) with a blot of something black to screw the
rainbow down to disconsolate pitch-such an out-and-out
burlesque of the real thing! But genuine Court grief
doesn’t run to a band or two of black pinned on for a day
or so just because everybody is wearing it; black silk or
some soft material is de rigueur—nb crepe is worn, and
you cease to let your grief well up, as it were, in your
wearing apparel at the expiration of a period (usually
three months) solemnly set forth in the Court Circular.'
While the princelets were here brother George ran
rings round his elder brother so far as popularity went
and yet if the former visited us again in his brand-new
capacity as next-mler-but-one to the throne of England,
Society would nrobably find its royal favourite quite a»
noli me tangere as his eldest brother was. It must be a
harassing position, that of a king in pickle, let out on a
chain with a reverend tutor at one end of it, and tanta-
lisingly accompanied by a frolicsome, unimportant, jol-
lier, nianlier-looking brother who can dance and flirt with
pretty well whom he likes, while the sacred youth fore-
doomed to the Crown must sit apart in gloomy grandeur
watching his pious tutor picking irreproachable partners
for him and minding all the pretty girls. Yes, regarding
him as an inoffensive kinglet who joyed innocently in
luminous linen, one feels rather sorry for the poor young
man, for his whole life was sapped and blighted by the
awful dignity of his national inheritance. If he was
(figuratively) kept in the same glass case as he was all the
time in Sydney, he must have been quite glad to get
away to a .better land.
Sydney Women’s College is to have as Lady Principal a
specially-imported Englishwoman, and the local girl-
graduates toiling along the thorny track of culture must
be greatly encouraged thereby. What is the good of our
women fagging the best years of their life away in pursuit
of top-grade book-learning if the only guerdon going is a
teachership in some suburban school or a governess-ship in
the dreary bush ? What is the use, I reiterate argumenta-
tively, of putting ice on your skull to keep your brains
from addling while you work up for your M.A, degree, if,
when a position opens out that is worth the risk and fag
of the thing, you are invariably set aside in favour of an
alien female who accepts the billet as if she was con-
ferring a favour, and possibly vaguely wonders what pro-
portion of the Sydney girl-students will be black gins ? Of
course—in the case of the Principalship just decided—the
Australian women who having “ minded their book” con-
sidered themselves fully’ competent for the position were
not debarred from sending their names in to the com-
mittee (in London) consisting of three English school-
marms, Sir Saul Samuel and someone else. But they
would have stood precisely the same chance if they had
referred their cases to the Sultan of Turkey.
One reason why Englishwomen have a “ walk-over ” for
all Australian educational sinecures is that sassiety—in
its out-reaehings after everything English—yearns for a
British lady’ of “tone” rather than a mother-know-all,
and the alleged want of “ finished manner ” about the
locally-produced lady pedagogue is the reason why she
isn’t a prophetess in her own country. But this is a
chimera. Sooner or later Australia will have a separate
national life and no more imported marionettes to give
object-lessons in “good breeding ” and how to eat gravy
with a fork, and the sooner we face our destiny and
behave according to our own lights, leaving drawn out A’s
and the “Piccadilly lurch” to their British originators,
the better. Why can’t we make our own good manners ?
Will Australians never recognise the vulgar aspect of
second-hand social tone ?
There’s a great deal of awed whispering on the subject
of the “delicacy and refinement of English habits” that
is hardly corroborated by the manners and customs of the
Briton when carefully’ observed. One Simonpure female
from across the pond was wont to pick her teeth with
her first finger—at the table, too. I have seen a “ noble”
Governor mimic a pianist before a crowded concert-room,
while his noble wife was convulsed with merriment
behind her lace fan ; and everybody has met and suffered
from the culchawed Hinglish young man who is cordially
made welcome by Australian hostesses, and returns their
kindnesses by 7 rude, shallow “criticisms” of a continent
to which nobody asked him to come, and in which
nobody’ would care how soon he left. These are just a
few samples of English manners on the jog, but perhaps
you don’t get the full bouquet of British “ tone ” —as it
were —unless the Briton’s foot is on his native heath. As
a rule he doesn’t seem to pack it for long sea voyages.
When a certain Governor took a recent flight some-
where in Barataria the train drew up at an inland station
thronged with the usual mob of inland sightseers. No
preparations had been made to adorn the station before
the Queen’s under study passed through, yet when that
distinguished individual loomed at the carriage- window
and began his flatteries his first remark was to thank them
for decorating the station in his honour. The crowd
thought it was a sarcastic allusion to the oversight, but
when the train moved away and they 7 “came to” the
mystery was soon solved. The signalman's red and green
flags were carelessly hung on the wall and the Governor
had mistaken them for loyal bunting !
There’s generally fun on the Border when the Customs
fiend is pawing his way’ through passengers’ luggage with
his chalk between his teeth, and so the other morning a
deeply-interested crowd gathered round the baggage of a
well-dressed man and woman who had come out of the
Sydney express and whom the Customs officer obviously
had his eye on. “ You’ll ’ave to pay jooty on all these,’’
he said, as he unearthed eleven brassy-looking teapots,
ten butter-dishes, and a forest of fish-slices in one trunk,
but the male owner sought to prove it wasn’t a smuggle.
“ They’re wedding-presents, yer know,” he said easily,
but if he expected that that bald statement would satisfy
the official he knew not his mark. The placid Border
detective only opened the lids of the eleven brassy tea-
pots and drew forth a bundle of cigars, gloves, and laces,
a nice little collection of durabilities altogether. “ We’ve
had the weddin’ prisint dodge before,” he said, senten-
tiously.
By the by, in South Australia—and all over this vast
continent for all I know (fiscal conditions are so puzzling)
—wedding presents are dutiable. A bride and bridegroom
recently whisked across the Border with a regular haul of
gifts, which were coldly appraised by the man with the
chalk, and the bridegroom—being in a whirl of bliss, 1
presume—shelled out his last farthing almost to pay the
duty. It was only when the honeymoon had waned that
he began to see that he had crippled hia income through
getting a square mile of jfiated, unnecessary utensils out
of pawn—but what is the good of venting your rage on
the cat when the thing’s done?
Riches have nasty little ways of taking unto themselves
wings occasionally—but some people oil their pinions in a
manner of writing. The latest alleged Juggins of the
mining exchange is an Australian man of inherited
millions who, it is whispered, has been a regular baa-lamb
among the broking black sheep of Collins-street West.
Not only plausible male speculators, brimming over with
golden scrip, but plungers in petticoats took a running
jump at the evening apotheosis of Tom Tiddler’s ground,
and one fair financier well-known all round “ let in ” this
confiding human gold-mine for £20,000 ! Some of her
dear friends were also put on to the golden goose, who
shed pen-feathers to the amount of £40,000. And the
consequence is—as the round game says—that the two
sons of this bamboozled dad will probably discover when
the will is read that to be born with a spoon in your
lantern jaws is no earthly assurance that the gilded ladle
is going to hang there for ever.
About Melbourne goes the whisper of the engagement
of a rich girl (now sojourning beside the Yarra) to a
wooden-looking Sydney militaire. The engagement is
kept a deep, dark mystery by the culprits concerned—on
account of the youth of the fiancee, they say—but every-
body is gradually getting to know all about it and
congratulations are flocking in.
That ghastly union between May and December entered
into not so long ago at an Australian country ohuroh, the
ceremony followed by a dejeuner at the local public-’
house, whence the guests fluttered to town by the last
train, leaving the happy turtle-doves to begin their
honeymoon among the ruins of the wedding-feast, isn’t
likely to last lonu. The ancient bridegroom is suffering
from dropsy, and grave hopes are entertained. Now, if
the venturesome bride has played the connubial cards
like a sportswoman, his demise will leave her one of the
wealthiest women in Australia.
Yours affectionately.
SAPPHO SMITH.
Cardinal Manning.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The heart of Labour throbs with pride and grief—
With pride to think that he, who now lies dead,
Stood in the workers' van—a valiant chief,
And bow’d before toil’s shrine his mitred head;
Ay, pride should be allied with grief to-day,
For princes now but seldom lead the poor,
but he was proud to walk the Master’s way
A princely priest of stainless life and pure.
He fought the People’s fight and won the love
Of those who hold the hig, broad faith in deeds,
That faith which makes for Right and leaps above
The bolts and bars and barriers of creeds,
That faith whose watchword is—“ God help the weak !
God help the friendless ones, and the distress’d !
God help ! God help all those who succour seek !”
This was his motto, who is now at rest!
From this new land we send a wreath of song—
A simple garland for his saintly bier—
The friend of justice and the foe of wrong,
His sacred mem’ry shall be treasur’d here—
And treasur’d, too, wherever heaven’s ray
Shall light the spark of brotherhood in man,
The splendid beacon of the coming day
When we shall have revealed God’s mighty plan.
Sydney.
THOMAS BRACKEN.
Two sharks caught at Chowder, Sydney,
last week, measured 14ft. 6in. (7ft. 2in. girth
and 16ft. (6ft. 2in. girth) respectively.
The decline of Freetrade in N.S.W.
Assembly has been very rapid during the last
decade. In 1885 the House numbered 4 Protec-
tionists ;in 1887, 42; 1889, 67; and in 1892, 75
out of a possible 141.
One gleans, from English notices, some
rather strange things about the early career of
Bishop Perry, formerly of Melbourne. He began
life as a freethinker, wherefore the good young
men of Cambridge were warned against him.
History again. Hare and Nicolson have
been writing absolutely contradictory accounts to
a Melbourne paper, although they are both police-
magistrates now, about* bushranger Power’s cap-
ture, and it only remains for police-superintendent
Montfort to give his—which he won’t, being a
modest man, as well as brave.
The pitiable spectacle presented by G. H.
Reid during the discussion which arose in the
N.S.W. Assembly the other evening, concerning
the question whether the new Sydney Post-office
frontages should be leased or sold, is a ghastly
illustration of the fate which sooner or later
inevitably overtakes every trimmer.
Those well-known Sydney artists, Messrs.
A. Collingridge and C. H. Hunt, have arranged
for the disposal of some of their pictures by art
union. The prizes number 32, and are valued at
£5OO, ranging in price from A 4 to £lOO, and com-
prising both oil-paintings and water-colour draw-
ings. The works are on view at the artists’
studio, 55 Norwich Chambers, Hunter-street.
“ Glorious News. Death of a pauper.
£50,000 per year saved out of the taxpayers'
earnings.” Such was the horror chalked on the
pavement, the other day, in front of the Trades
Hall, Melbourne, and of course people are always
most angry when you tell them their own
thoughts. Therefore did the minion of Murphy,
Trenwith, and Hancock rush for a mop.
The Duke of Fife —the ambitious Macbeth
was Thane of Fife —begins to loom up as a
dangerous-looking personage. Just imagine his
feelings towards Prince George, how anxiously he
craves and prays that this beloved Prince may
never have typhoid again ! Why is it expedient
to place such fearful temptations in the way of
any human creature? General Kellermann said,
“ Bonaparte saw the French Crown lying on the
ground, and picked it up with the point of his
sword.” Such prospects kill all natural feeling,
an experience which is one of the Republican
foundations.
Parkes foams at the slightest reference to
the “ Whereat ” episode in his parliamentary life.
Ten years ago, nearly, Parkes was kicked out of
East Sydney; and he was obliged to fly from the
hustings before the enraged populace, and seek an
asylum in the house of a Hebrew, in Castlereagh-
street. No constituency would have him at any
price ; and but for the grace of Whereat, the only
candidate for Tenterfield, Parkes would have had
to seek a refuge in the Council. The trick on the
Tenterfield electors was worked very cunningly.
Not until it was too late to run an opposition
candidate, did Whereat retire and apply his forty
sovereigns to nominate Parkes—to the confusion
of the constituency. And Parkes is indignant
because he once owed a seat to the pity of a
protectionist!
Sir Samuel Griffith, the British diplomat,
is dragging the Queensland Premiership in the
dirt. Under the plausible plea of reviving inter-
provincial interest in his Federation fad he ia
quietly button-holing the Premiers of N.S.W. and
victoria, theleadingprovinces,on the chances, they
say, of extracting a bigger subsidy towards the cost
of the protectorate of New Guinea. With what
object ? To put the finishing touch on his recent
mission to New Guinea ! The red-letter day is
fast nearing—only a year or two off - when no
black-labor can be longer worked on the Queens-
land plantations. And with a view to robbing
the white-labor vote Griffith has arranged with
Sir W. Macgregor to receive an influx of sugar
planters, under specially favoured conditions ; and
he is now negotiating for an increased vote all
round the provinces in aid of the Queensland
Black-labor Employing Association. Will the
Anstralias sanction the revival of niggerdom
within coo-ey of their shores? And if Queens-
land vetoes the production of sugar with black-
labor will it subsidise a protectorate which affords
a haven to blackleg Queensland planters?
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
the Country Milk Oo.’s “ Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
To the inventive genius of a lady in 1843
are we indebted for the first ice-cream freezer,
but those ladies who wish to save time, labour,
and money invest in the new German Freezer.
Why ? There is no handle to turn, not half so
much ice used, and it keeps the cream firm in or
out doors. Two quarts of ice cream can be made
for Is. fid. to Is. fid. Send for Sydney testimonials
to T. A. Knight & Co., St. James Chambers,
King-street, Sydney, who supply the machine.
Price, 305., with instructions so simple that a
child can understand.

The Theatre Royal continues to be the
best-filled house in Sydney, and weather or n 0
weather “The Forty Thieves” appear to be a
force strong enough to overflow the treasury. The
pantomime would, however, stand a little bright-
ening up. The gags are growing mildewed, and
the songs are becoming decrepid. Fred. Mason’s
coster sketch is growing musty, and Rose Dear-
ing’s whistling song commences to lack the
inspiration of an encore verse. One of the
brightest glimpses of the show is the seductive
Dora Green well-like business of the “Fairy Four,”
whose dainty quartette of “Hush-a-by Baby,”
and the school-girl ballet, are worthily provoca-
tive of the muse of the poet whose Chief delight is
in histrionic faery-land, only, unfortunately, he
doesn’t live in our street. Mr. Gordon’s scenery
is one of the things that do not stale ; but did he
paint the tableaux introducing the transformation
scene ? They consist of four pictures illustrative
of incidents in the life of Siegfried as interpreted
by the musician of the future—Richard Wagner—
and for eight more such pictures we would gladly
forgo the transformation-scene itself, the harle-
quinade, and the entire libretto of the “ pankv-
mine. ”
* *■ * *
The Christmas edition of Maggie Moore,
in Lincoln - green with trimmings, meanders
delectably at Her Majesty’s, and many things
transpire of no particular interest to the general
public at the same place of amusement. One of
the radiant entities that show up vividly against
a black back-ground of impossible villainy, is the
shadow-dance of Bella Bash all, which, while
reflecting no little credit on the lady in question,
brings into strong relief the omnipotence of the
principal performer in all the pieces staged at this
particular theatre—-to wit, the immortal man who
works the limelight, whose genius has saved a
myriad shows, and whose effulgent efforts have
redeemed numerous tanks of real water, and saved
the reputation of many a blood-stained Agin-
court. The wonderful sensibilities of the stage-
moon, which ever dogs the footsteps of the star
who draws the biggest salary, were never so happily
exemplified as on the boards of Her Majesty’s, and
it’s only just that the unseen genius who works all
the most striking effects should be suitably
acknowledged. Geo. P. Carey has recovered
sufficiently to undertake the small inebriate part
of Friar Tuck, and be does it so well that we are
sorry he is not amassing wealth in a more serious
personation. Outside Maggie Moore, everybody
is trying to do his or her best—with that lady
herself it comes natural, and calls for no special
remark.
** * *
At the Criterion the “ Mikado ” wuc to
a rainy conclusion on the 16th inst., and was suc-
ceeded on Monday last with a two-nights revival of
the “Pirates of Penzance.” On Wednesday,
“ Patience ” was produced, for the first time iu
Sydney by this company, and we should be sorry
indeed that they had gone away, were it not for
the fact that their vacation of the boards heralded
the return of the Brough-Boucicault Comedy
Company, which appears to-night (Saturday) in
“ Uncles and Aunts.” Young Alf. Stephens has
shown an ability in burlesque-acting and a versa-
tility incharacterisation which should bean earnest
of something worth-while to come, only we are
reminded, by the case of the historic Master
Betty’s failure to realise the promise of his youth,
that precocity is anything but a guarantee of
mature success. Little Lily Stephens also is the
one juvenile maid out of a pretty numerous bevy
who abuts considerably into the realms of our
perception, and we are rather glad on behalf of
the susceptible Sydney youth, that she lacks
several years of dangerous seventeen.
** * *
“ Uncles and Aunts,” now staged for the
first time in Australia, is a farcical comedy by W.
Leatocq and Walter Everard, and Tom Cahnam
and Charles Fabert, two comedians lately
imported from England, have been announced as
additional allurements. Brough himself super-
vises the season, and he announces the production
of “ New Lamps for Old,” “ The Solicitor,” “The
Barrister,” and a genuine collection of comedy
novelties.
** * *
The Polytechnic is now drawing fairly
well, and on the 16th—it is alleged, inconsequence
of the imposition of the new tariff duties—Felix
Tanner began in this neatly-furnished catacomb
the interesting experiment of living on air, water,
tobacco-smoke and irrelevant conversation for
fifty-one days. The air, conversation and tobacco-
smoke are almost synonymous items, and are sup
plied free of cost by the frequenters to this cheer-
ful vault. Mr. E. Lloyd Lewis has temporarily
abandoned clairvoyancy and conjuring, and his
place on the stage is occupied by a fighting
kangaroo and a skating maniac.

George Musgrove’s grand opera scheme
doesn’t seem likely to fetch them in Melbourne,
where a limited meeting of music patrons sup-
norted the motion in a despondent key and sug-
gested that times were bad. Even if the guarantee
monev were raised in each city, and Muzzy
brought Sir ’Gustus’s show along, the chances are
that Melbourne would find the singing no better,
if not weaker, than that of the unfortunate
Siraonsen company, which are just now out of
business. Simonsen’s people can give a fane
operatic performance and might have been another
Bernhardt boom at the Princess’, under William-
son’s management. Instead of which they were
produced in the wrong operas, at the wrong
theatre, in a style so mean and paltry as to create
a suspicion that they couldn’t be half as good as
they seemed.

A series of promenade concerts will be
given in Sydney Centennial Hall on Saturday
(j an- 23rd), Monday, and Tuesday. The pro-
gramme is long, the list of artistes is formidable,
and contains a multitude of good names, and the
selections are of a kind calculated to soothe even a
Goth or a Vandal. Mr. Walter J. Turner
officiates as musical director.
#* * *
The talented Greenwood family have taken
the Royal Standard Theatre, Castlereagh-street,
Sydney, for January 2G, and 28, and will, with
competent professional assistance, give on these
dates some high-class variety entertainments, after
which they think of visiting the provinces.
** * *
“Dick Whittington ” booms along at Mel*
bourne Royal quite regardless of the Age boycott,
and the free-list is still haughtily suspended
“ except to members of the press on duty. ” The
leading daily oracle has no member on duty. Pro-
prietor Syme waves his paws, like Podsnap of old,
and refuses to recognise the possibility of a panto-
mime existing without his august approval. Every
other Melbourne show receives the sanction of the
Age two or three times a week, but the name of
the Roval has been wiped off the books of that
enraged journal. Even the advertisement columns
breathe no mention of “Dick Whittington’s”
presence in the land, and the great Coppin
spectacle is in much the same position as a late-
lamented Roman citizen who was considered to be
especially honoured by the omission of his bust
from a public display of heads of the people. In
short, the Age, by striving too hard to avenge
the expulsion of its deadheads, has made a huge
ass of itself.
* # *
“Dick Whittington ” is advertised by the
beauty of Brunton’s scenery, and the lavish
magnificence of a pageant which probably sur-
passes anything of the kind previously put upon
Melbourne Royal stage. Furthermore, it is re-
markable for elegance and skill in the dancing
department, and a few other matters. The
beauteous Billeo Barlow has always fetched them
with two of her best music-hall ditties, and now
that a melancholy event prohibits any loyal
reference to the Duke of Clarence’s marriage,
Billee’s popularity will become more marked.
Lilian Davis is another festive young person,
whose legs speak for themselves, whilst George
Walton occasionally pops up to special advantage
amidst a lot of capable performers. An artist
like Edwin Kelly was last week playing the very
minor part of old man Fitzwarren in somebody’s
stead.
* -* * *
The Alexandra pantomime has nearly
reached the end of its tether. A benefit to the
author of that short-lived annual was to come off
on Tuesday, and his work will come off altogether
after Saturday next (23rd), leaving the rival enter-
tainment to enjoy a bloated monopoly of good
fairies and coloured fire. “Jack the Giant-killer
has drawn fairly good houses, but it never looked
like a genuine stayer from the first.
** * *
“The Merry Monarch ” is likewise retir-
ing from the throne in disgust. At the outset he
earned a bad character for gilded stupidity and
flashes of dreariness, and this detrimental cele-
brity has seemingly stuck to the potentate, despite
his subsequent endeavours to improve. He lias
many good points about him, indeed. The way
in which he goes for foreign embassies with a
singlestick is worthy of all praise, and his large-
hearted manner of assisting an elopement in high
life proven him to be every inch a king. One way
and another the M.M. and his nautchy ballet have
established a solid claim to a revival at some
future time. Elton, however, is said to be
actually going away anon, on which account the
management announces a round of William’s last
appearances in favourite operas prior to the pro-
duction of “ La Cigale ” and the new prima donna.
On Saturday he is to appear as Polydoro in “The
Old Guard.”
*» * *
“ Much Ado about Nothing” seems to be
holding its own at Melbourne Bijou. The ground
floor of the pretty little house is generally
crowded, and the dress-circle supports the Bard
very freely, considering that his simple narrative
is not exactly fin de sieclc. It can’t be said that
the performance throws new light on the character
of any immortal creation, or explains whether
Benedick was really struck on Beatrice before the.
play started, or Beatrice intended to “bunch ”
Benedick under any circumstances. The possible
state of Hero’s feelings towards Claudio at the
end of a long honeymoon with that hysterical
young man is nob suggested by any subtle inflec-
tion of the voice, as far as one can hear, nor does
Anson give such a masterly reading of the im-
mortal text as to make it certain that Dogberry's
maternal aunt ever thought of running away
with the footman. The B. and B. company give
a plain, straightforward interpretation of the
comedy, taking Shakespeare as he comes. Some-
times he comes a bit heavy, but the scenery pulls
him through, and his worst witticisms are fortu-
nately too old to be understood.
* * ! M
Sells’ Circus draws its eight or te 11
thousand people a day down to the gardens
Richmond, In a general way the show is con-
sidered immense, although the hypercritical find
fault with it for not being quite as marvellous as
the posters. The hippopotami, for instance, simply
trot round a dry track instead of swimming up
the circus in fifteen fathoms of river, and the
other wild animals as a rule forget to show all the
teeth they display in the coloured picters. On
the other hand, though, the Bedouin Arabs make
more row than might be expected, and the clown
elephant sometimes throws in an extra piece of
entertainment by swallowing the bottle after-
taking a drink. The circus will fold itself up and
steal ponderously away on Friday of next week.
** * *
The talented Japs, from distant Tokio,
wherever that may be, are attracting attention at
the Gaiety Hall, and some other shows remain as
usual. At the Exhibition an International Tug of
War was started on Monday, under wide-awake
management. About fourteen different nations
or provinces are represented in this struggle for
supremacy, and as each nation has to tug the
other nations until it is beaten four times the
chances are that they will all be tugging for the
six days of this week. Why the nations should
thus furiously rage together and pull opposite
ways is not made quite clear, but the admission is
one shilling, and reserved seats are Is. 6d. extra.
The advantage of having a reserved seat is that
you can see the perspiration dropping off the
nation’s brows and catch the several accents of
their grunts.

At Melbourne Bijou next Saturday after-
noon (23rd) there will be a strong all-round
performance for the benefit of Mrs. G. B. W.
Lewis, who is about to bid Australia a long-
adieu.
At Poverty Point.
An indignant Sydneyite writes to a southern paper
complaining of Maloom's being left out of a list of the old
circuses. Malcom was indeed the Sydney Hengler, and
bought the Ark from Noah. Few people sp-e aware that
he seated his pit with the boards, while others made the
baulks for his horses, and his myall pipe was turned from
the lid of the window which let out the dove.
As soon as ever a man is appointed an auditor, he
becomes mentally paralysed. We turn up the 40-year-old
case of Walter Walts, in London, and And that everyone
agreed that complete audit reform must ensue. Watts,
a mere under-clerk in the Globe Insurance Co., embezzled
£70,000, in six years, applying the money to theatrical
speculations at the Marylebone and Olympic Theatres ;
he hanged himself at last in Newgate. It was he who
brought out Mrs. Warner, at the Marylebone, in Shake-
spearian rivalry to Phelps, of Sadlers Wells, and he
fetched out the Yankee, Anna Cora Mouatt, in style, with
her lovely illuminated grotto dressing-room, under the
stage.
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis complimentarily benefits at
Melbourne Bijou, on her departure for England,
This is Rose Edouin, going home to see brother Willie,
after educating ever so many clever people in the way
they should go on the stage. We don’t suppose Sells’
circus will turn up in commemoration of G. B. W. Lewis
having opened Astley’s Amphitheatre, Melbourne, so long
afterwards known as the Princess’.
Bland Holt is in full swing at Hobart Royal but not
doing the “ biz.” of twelve months ago—the reality of
some of his sensational scenes is just a little bit too funny
—lie put on a blowing-up bit the other night and
frightened the audience out of twelve months growth
and made them think the Judgment had come at last.
The Taylor-Carrington Dramatic Company has worked
its way to good biz. as far as Rockhampton with “Barnes
of New York ” in tow.
Organist Jude writes from Brisbane to say that
he had the reverse to empty houses at Maryborough,
where he did very well indeed. He reports capital
business as far up as Townsville, and states that his organ-
recitals in Brisbane drew large crowds. From Brisbane
he goes to Maoriland and Tasmania, and hopes to spread
organic culture through 41 towns.
The MacMahons have done big business at Sandhurst
and Ballarat with “Evangeline.” “The Corsair,” too,
was considered a satisfactory leg-show in the mining
districts, so the brethren would seem to be in luck again.
Simonsen talks of bringing out another tragic prima
donna in place of the invalided Guidotti, and giving his
Italian Opera Company another chance. The relations
between lit tle Martin and his principals are not uncordial,
seeing that all salaries were paid up to a week or so
before the season closed, and the people who could least
afford to wait received a quittance in full. In the mean-
time there should be little difficulty about keeping such
a fine lot of singers together on the concert lay.
The gorgeous Eastern dresses in “ Dick Whittington ”
were sold to the Melbourne Royal agent, in London, by
poor Mrs. Langtry, at a genuinely alarming sacrifice.
They were built for the famous “ Antony and Cleopatra”
show, which collapsed before their glory had time to
fade.
Plain Bill Holloway has secured the Australian rights
of “Gloriana.”
Bateman and O’Grady’s dramatic company have been
doing splendid business in VV.A. Fitzgerald’s circus was
crammed at high prices, not only in the city, but in the
country. Tommy Hudson arrives shortly from India.

Everywhere he is recognised as a line actor, treading

Williamson is satisfied he has a tramp-card in Miss
Halton, prima donna, just to hand, consigned by H. H.
Vincent, “with care,” &c. Her training was French, her
artistic successes have mainly been American, though
she has the London stamp too. So “La Oigale ” rattles
in rehearsal, and will be a big draw, though the newest
thing, “La Basoche,” is better. J. C. W. had “La
Cigale ” planned for Xmas, but wouldn’t do it without a
prima donna.

worked up a ben. and attended to the Sydney strandhngs
first.

Florence St. John and Marius appear to have been

Fancy sentiment of this kind at the Pelican !

Aimiie Moore, lady journalist, dramatic light of the
Austral Salon, announces she is going on the stage, after
the frantic acclaims bestow ed on her Juliet in the balcony
scene. Poor little things!—a lot they know about the
stage, with their bits of balcony scene, which are to hoist
them into Essie Jenynses right off. Look at Gwen. Floyd,
after her big hit in “ The Dancing Girl,” and here she is,
at the same theatre, doing a wretched leg-part of a few'
lines in the pantomime, while impudent Lilian Davies
takes off all the honours. Gwen, hasn't even learnt to
high kick.
At last advices, Johnel Sullivan was very much boozed
in ’Frisco, and was pursuing his theatrical career
under difficulties. The audiences were small, and Johnel,
as the blacksmith, insisted on shaking his hammer at
them, and w 7 anted badly to “put a tail” on somebody.
Also, he took to falling asleep standing, and most of the
time he forgot bis part. Consequently, when he ought to
have uttered some noble and manly sentiment, he only
looked about with a drunken glare and said, “ Blast them
newspapers and when he should have been rushing to
the rescue of somebody in distress, he generally lurched
to the footlights and offered promiscuously to fight any-
body or everybody for forty million dollars, and the
heroine in distress had to rescue herself the best way she
could.
