In case any misconception may arise con-
cerning a letter from a correspondent, - printed in
last issue of The Bulletin in connection with the
alteration in the composition of the old-established
firm of Gordon and Gotch, it is as well to state
that the firm will continue, as heretofore, to trade
under the old title which is so extensively and
favorably known throughout Australasia.
A young fellow was the other day arrested
at Forbes (N.S. W.) on a charge of deserting an
illegitimate child, and was taken in handcuffs to
Albury, 200 miles distant. When confronted
with the child and mother, the latter shrieked out
that this was not the man, whereupon he was let
go. “ But I’ll bo the man if you like,” said the
ill-used suspect: and a few days saw him leaving
Albury with a wife and an adopted child. And
this happened within the magistracy of “Rolf
Boldrewood,” the novelist. Not many wives come
per warrant and handcuffs.
The impending strike in the Northern
collieries of N.S.W. will just about damn the
Newcastle coal-trade, as matters arc at present.
Queensland, Japan, and other coal-producing
centres will chip in for all they are worth, and as
a reliable export place Newcastle will be dis-
credited. A countervail to this injury may be
found in so fostering N.S. Wales industries, that
as much of its coal output as possible may be pro-
fitably utilised locally. In this direction Protec-
tion may yet be the saviour of the N.S. Wales
coal-trade. In respect of the dispute itself, it
will always stand out prominently that the
associated coal-masters took the first ugly step by
giving notice of a reduction in the hewing-rate
by a re-adjustment of the sliding-scale. The men
tried to arrange matters specially with the non-
associated masters, who certainly had a chance to
“collar” the market, but these
failed. All the masters are sticking together to
reduce the hewing-rate from 4s. 2d. to 3s. 10d..
their ostensible excuse being that they have been
enamelled to reduce the selling-price.
HIS “BOOK.”
he wrote the book of the panto.,
Evolved all its rollicking fun ;
'Twas he did the topical ditty
Thai’s whistled and sung through the city-
lie’s Prince of the Primitive Pun.
He wrote the book of the panto.,
His wife has just borne him a son ;
They say that she swears at his wheezes,
They say that at home he just freezes—
But the panto, will have a big run.
He wrote the book of the panto.,
The curtain’s rung up, it’s begun,
There’s a joker who jokes like a martyr,
And his wife, who’s a terrible Tartar,
With a fist that weighs nearly a ton.
He wrote the book of the panto.—
His child has a terrible cough,
And he says, when you call his book funny,
“ Yes, hang it, for I’ll get no money
Till the panto, is finally off.”

Personal Items.
Colonel “ Tom ” Price is, it appears, a
nephew of Sir John Franklin.
How many people know that Lady Jersey
is the Zenobia in “ Endymion ?”
Dailey used to read “CalJista,'’ the tine
novel by Cardinal Newman, through once-a-year.
Wadham Wyndham, the homicidal crank
now in Darlinghurst prison, is a blood-relation of
the Knatchbulls.
The late Cardinal Mannings own words
should be inscribed upon his tomb : “ Every man
has a right to bread or to work.”
Seventeen volumes of stories and novels,
excluding Rolf Boldrewood’s, were published bv
alleged Australian authors in London last year. “
A volume of poems by “Philip Dale”
(Mrs. Cyril Haviland) and Cyril Haviland, the
secretary to Sydney School of Arts, is announced'
for publication in London.
Rudyard Kipling has irretrievably offended
the Yankees by this sentence in “His. Private
Honor : “ Ortheris, being neither a menial nor
an American, but a free man, had no excuse for
yelping.”
G. E. Labertouche, who has done ten
months out of his five years for the Imperial
1 ensions frauds at S\ 7 dney, is, it is said, to have
a license given him at the end of one year’s im-
prisonment.
When Archibald Michie, Q.C. (Vic.), wei t
to London, it was remarked that he, Cardinal
Manning and John Stuart Mill might pass for
brothels, so much were they alike. -Michie is now
the only one of the trio left.
Said the rich and arrogant David Syme
once to a reporter : “ That is not the sort of stuff
which made the Age. We want fire, not froth.”
Yes, said the poor reporter- thoughtfully—
whisky is better than beer.”
The new N.T. Government Resident in
succession to J, G. Knight, deceased, will not be
a local man. The general opinion amongst
Adelaide people is that the position will be offered
to J. M. Stuart, the Crown Prosecutor.
Mr. Tiger Inglis informed the N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly the other evening that he
was by profession a planter. Mr. ex-Undertaker
Melville modestly coughed—a hint that he had
been in a somewhat similar occupation himself.
ft is believed that Premier Munro will
appoint his present secretary, Ebenezer Thomas
secretary to the Agent-General, and raise the
salary from £BOO to £ 1000 a year. How the
“ next in rotation ” in London will love Thomas !
“ Moyarra,” advertised as “a new poem
on an Australian aboriginal subject,” is by G. W.
Rusden, the Australian author suspected of being
in Sir Robert Walpole’s mind when he alleged the
general unveracity of historians. It was first
published in 1851.
Allan Macpherson, formerly M.L.A. for
Central Cumberland, N.S.W,, died recently in
Scotland. His legislative career was chiefly
noticeable for a bout at fisticuffs on the floor of
the House with another member—we think Mr.
Benjamin Lee, now S.M.
Cardinal Manning's marriage was with
Miss Sargent, a clergyman’s daughter, whose
Sisters married Soany Sam Wilberforee and his
brother Henry. Soapy’s two brothers became
Catholics, and altogether he was in a terrible stew
■about his own preferment.
W. K. Thomson is visible again about
Melbourne, and is heartily welcomed. W.K.T.
is a fair and square man of honour, who got a
knockdown financial blow, and the mercantile
world subscribed <£lo,ooo for his wife and family
—a thing absolutely unique in Australia.
Stanley, on leaving the Australia Hotel,
Sydney, wrote in the visitors’ book :

An Australian journalist had a collection
of about four hundred bits of verse he had from
tune to time contributed to the Melbourne and
Adelaide weekly press. After keeping them for
some years by him, a fa Horace, he thought he
would revise them for publication in book form.
Going over them, he burnt three “pomes” as
unworthy of reprint, and, giving the others to his
wite, told her to destroy all in her judgment
unsuitable for reproduction. She burnt the lot.

He may thank the initial puff which Mr. Glad-

The other day in the Transvaal two youno
British subjects, Cooper and M‘Keone, were tried
before the Chief Justice for robbing Kaffirs. One
ot the prisoners was a iad of under 20 ; the other
was 2b. They were undefended, and had not the
slightest suspicion that their lives were in danger
but on their conviction the Chief Justice dis-
covered that by the Dutch Common Law highway
robbery may be punished by death, and he put
tiie old law into force. When the last mail left
Capetown the two men were to be hanged on the
following day, but we have not heard how the
matter ended.

A well-known writer some time ago sent
an Australian story to the brightest and si might
best weekly paper in America. The editor wrote
back : ‘ Your story I have read with interest and
pleasure, but we can’t print it, for the American
people don’t care a damn what happens in Aus-
traha. They don’t know Australia and don't
want to know it.” So much for the “ Federation
of the English-speaking races.” The Americans’
business is chiefly in America.
(< Never call your enemy “ a thiefcall him
‘ a damned thief,” and you will escape with small
damages. In London, recently, a man who
attended a meeting of creditors got so riled at
certain revelations that he called a solicitor “»
damned thief of a lawyer. This was just what
Ins costs-ship wanted and an action was entered for
slander. Mr. Justice Day directed the jury that
the words quoted were not actionable. “If (said
Mi. Justice Day) the solicitor had simply been
called a thief of a lawyer,’ it would have been
quite another affair, because the omission of the
word damned would have shown that the
defendant was not in a passion, but merely
abusive. ’ Of course, the solicitor appealed, but
the Master of the Rolls, Lord Justice Lopes and
Lord Justice Kay dismissed the appeal, and ruled
hat whereas thief ” was actionable, “damned
tmef would pass. This is cheering news to
Australians generally and a perfect throw-in for
Queenslanders.
The Mechanism of Man.
A man has within him a working pump called his
heart, a working bellows called his lungs, a work
lr *n 9 a^ ec l stomach, a workinsr condenser
called his brain, and a working evaporator called
his skin, all of which must be at work whether he
will or not. The heart is expending over his
body day by day, 122 foot-tons of work. He will
go on lifting so many millions of tons in so many
years. If he meet with no accident, the time
j , come . wh ® n his las t stroke will be finished
and he will die, or he may be carried off in
earlier hte by disease, which, however slight in
Its early stages, works like a ferment. The
symptoms may be scarcely noticeable—a little
laziness, slight headaches or, backaches—but
the cause is always at work. Take a handsome
rosy-cheeked young man, full of life and hope ; let
a germ of disease enter his system ; it is invisible,
too small to be weighed ; judged by the evidence
of the senses it is too insignificant to be thought
of, but it extends indefinitely. It may be months,
it may be years, but if the symptoms are neglected
the deadly disorder will do its tVal work; the
step loses its elasticity, the eyes become dull, the
cheeks pale, strength departs, and eventually the
joyous youth is but a shell, a cadaverous, shrunken
form, and death ends the dreadful scene. With
such facts staring us daily in the face how careful
ought we to be that the first warning voice of
nature should be heeded, and that when we suffer
those tired, aching feelings, those nervous head-
aches, neuralgia, and other signs of approaching
catastrophe we should have trust in that great
regenerator of vital strength Clements Tonic, which
positively restores pristine health to every organ
of the body, as instanced in the following case
In justice to the value of Clements Tonic 1 wish
to add my testimony to its efficacy. My daughter
has for several years been afflicted with torturing
headaches and complete prostration, for which
she tried numerous remedies to no purpose. At
length a friend sent her a bottle of Clements
Tonic, and a few doses gave such relief that she
will hear of no other remedy ; and other persons
in my household, who have been similarly afflicted,
have the same faith in its properties, W, E.
Murtagh, Mitta Mitta, Vic.” The above was
declared before me at Mitta Mitta, in the
Northern Bailiewick of Victoria, this 2nd day
of October, 1891, John Conway, J.P.

has been agreed by the West Australian Legisla-
tive Council that every person apparently under
the age of 16 years summarily convicted of any
offence of larceny, embezzlement, or aiding, &c.,
shall, in addition to, or in lieu of, any other
punishment, be flogged.

The great feature of the East Adelaide
election canvass is the way the women are working
for M’Pherson, the Labour leader. The meeting
in Adelaide Town Hall was visited by Mrs. Mary
Lee and a contingent of the Working Women’s
Trades Union, who were loudly cheered. M'Pher-
son is a rattling speaker and a clever politician,
who has been of enormous service to the Labour-
party.

man being identified with Labour would not, as
heretofore, militate against the possibility of his
appointment. The Trades and Labour Council
should be content with this.

Ballarat, has decided to stand by his bleeding
country, and offer himself for re-election. He
was enjoying broken health in Auckland when the

back. Whereupon the seasoned warrior got

Parkes says he, “ like Peel,” was converted
by Cobden. Rubbish. As The Bulletin has
already pointed out, Peel was really converted by
the Irish famine—if a change necessitated by
expediency can be called a conversion. When ic
repealed the Corn Laws, Peel was forced to favour
Freetrade in general even though he probably am
not believe in it. The son of a Lancashire manm
facturer, he could not, as the resistance of Disraeli
afterwards proved, in a house of landlords injure
the protected agricultural interests while leaving
his own class untouched.

As was bound to be the case sooner or
later, respecting something or other, the Queens
land Government and the Queensland Civil-
Service Board have come into a deadlock collision.
The Under-sheriff died, and while the Civil-
Service Board has the nomination and supposed
appointment in its own power, the Minister for
Justice has in his hands the endorsement, without
which the appointment is not complete, ihe
Minister wanted to run up Chief Bailiff Galleyey,
but the board appointed C. P. b. Boyce. Ihe
Minister refuses to confirm the appointment—
and, at the time of writing, the trouble is at full
head.
Goldwin Smith, in delivering what he
declared would be his last political utterance from
a Canadian platform, spoke thus about Canada s
union with the United States ; •
The sand in my hour-glass is low ; and, before it quite
runs out, there are a few things gathered during a
student’s life which I should like, if I can, to put in shape.
Let my parting words be to urge you to look tacts steadily
in the face. Canada can afford to drift no longer.
Things may go on for a long time, but at last the inevit-
able will come, and it will not then be an equal and
honourable union ; it will be annexation indeed.
These pregnant sentences were aroused by the
rampant Amerioanophobia of Canadian and
British Jingoism.
Australia is sorry for the death of Prince
Albert Victor, because it is full of good-natured
people who always regret to hear that any father
and mother, even the veriest strangers, have lost
their son, or that any girl has lost her sweetheart.
But Australia has seen a cablegram stating that
LondJi Times “ regards the universal grief dis-
played in the colonies at the sad event, as witness-
ing the strength of the links of Empire, &0., and
it resents the effort made by the enemies of Aus-
tralian Nationalism to use, as political proofs and
arguments, the expressions of regret and sympathy
which, at best, can only be construed as the out-
come of civilized courtesy or diplomatic formality.
General bchaff, at the Episcopal church
Congress, Washington, U.S.A. : “What a glaring
lack of integrity our country is every day display-
ing here in Washington, stamping 80 cents worth
of silver with its great hand and calling it 10U,
and having the effrontery to say, ‘ln God we
trust. 5 ” This misses the real point. After all,
the act is performed by the nation for its own use
and recognition, and, if it is a national sin, it is
nationally paid for and nationally expiated, .but
apply a parallel argument to the private banks in
Australia permitted to issue paper I O Us that
are legal tender, in a proportion to bullion
altogether unwarranted by reasonable estimates
of solid solvency, and a vista is opened indeed of
monopolied power that is an audacious privilege
against democratic rights and possessions.
If G. H. Reid had any real democratic
grit, he would challenge the Dibbs Government
concerning their appointment of Scourger Glory
to the governorship of Deniliquin (N.S. W.) Gaol,
which is an outrage on the conscience of a tree
people. Is Reid too much of an official to see
that the Government argument that Ellory, being
a penal department official, is entitled to his pro-
motion like other warders of his own seniority, is
a sham ? Ellory was made a senior warder because
he agreed to flog. When he became scourger he
committed social suicide. In one hand he weighed
the emoluments of his position as Government
manflayer; in the other he held the social
ignominy which as an intelligent—we regret to
say Ellory is an intelligent man —he knew was
attached to the position. The Bulletin hopes to
see every self-respecting man in the_N.S.W. penal
department strike rather than remain in a, service
which gives effect to the shameful doctrine that
promotion depends on the performance of offices
disgusting to civilised man.

M.L.A. Crick has become a “sport” and
now owns a horse called the Snark. Memo, for

The new Duke of Bedford, whose father
the second richest peer of the realm, committed
suicide just a year ago under singularly sensational
circumstances, has an absolute horror of being seen
by strangers; and always dines in solemn state at
midnight, though he is an unusually early riser.
When the Benvenue was wrecked recently
off Sandgate (Eng.) the captain died like a
philosopher of the olden time. About ten minutes
before the final crash he lit his pipe and sat down
peacefully in his cabin, and when the ship broke
in two and went down he went with her, smoking
tranquilly to the last. And now he is a skeleton
with a pipe at the bottom of the English Channel,
and he and his unfinished smoke drift about
together amid the seaweed and the sands.
When Babylon went down two per cent,
of her people owned her entire wealth. When
Persia went down one per cent, of her population
owned all of her wealth. When Rome went down
one thousand eight hundred men owned and con
trolled the known world. In 1800, sixty-three
and one-half per cent, of the people owned the
entire wealth of the U.S. In 1870, thirty-seven
per cent, of the people owned the entire wealth of
the U.S. In 1890, twenty per cent, of the people
owned the entire wealth of the States.
Sydney Mobnino Hebald says of Albert
Victor, deceased :
Happily for all, the young Prince has left behind him
the record of a blameless youth ; and notwithstanding
the fierce light that heals upon a throne and everyone
surrounding it, there has not been a scandal or even 11’-
report to sully his memory.
Thus is history made ! The Herald here reminds
us of the Gbaphic, which, in printing a life of
Albert Edward, left out the Mordaunt and bacca-
rat incidents.
The alleged “ Dora-Dora blacks,” who are
wanted for a murder committed something like a
year ago in Victoria, still crop up occasionally in
the papers. The police have never once seen
them, but they have followed the tracks of four
naked feet right through Victoria and across more
than half N.S. Wales, and how often they have
lost the original feet and picked up a fresh set no
one knows. The only man who saw the two
indubitable niggers—the ones with the trade-mark
on them and the name blown in the buttle—-is
dead months ago, owing to a spear in his back;
and if the police ever capture the pair—or any
other pair—they must either let them go again or
hang them without evidence. If they meant to
let them go, then we presume they wouldn’t run
after them, so evidently they intend to hang
them, anyhow. According to latest advices,
the missing heathens were near Tamworth.
from which it is conjectured that they
must have fled down Oxford-street, Sydney,
and across Gebrge-street, and made for the north
by running over the wastes of Pyrmont and
Five Dock, or else they came in by train on an
aboriginal pass, blindly granted by some Govern-
ment official down south, and escaped by omnibus
from Redfern. The telegram further states that
in one day they ran 50 miles over mountains,
rivers, and fences, with the police a good second,
and the proceedings closed as usual with the
niggers one mile ahead. They always are ahead,
and the way the chase will end is a foregone con-
clusion. The two blackfellows will scoot right
ahead till they go round the world, and as they
are doing their second lap, they will come up on
the police still engaged on their first one, and
will run over them and break their backs. This
is the only real danger of the case, and the officers
are hereby enjoined to keep a bright look-out
behind.

writing of this intensely interesting work. ... No
newspaper notice, however full, can give any adequate
impression of the career as is here depicted of the great
Irishman who has gone. Mr. O’Connor’s hook itself should
be in the hands of everyone who is interested in the
brilliant and stormy career which is ended for ever. . .
A delightful, graphic and biographical sketch of one of
the most remarkable figures of the century.”—Dublin
Freeman's Journal.

Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each,
individual to live upon, but it has been loft to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co. to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
work's at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A’. Knight and Co., St.

Society
Loyalty is the art of lying over dead
princes.
* * *
When do the bad dead princes die 1 We
have read the obituaries of a good many, and
apparently the Lord wasn’t a patch on any one of
them.
* * #
Thus the cable: “Cardinal Manning, when
dying, exclaimed ‘ Thank God ’ on being told that
the Duke of Clarerjce was slightly better.” Now,
why did the dying man say merely “ Thank God?”
Because the Duke was only slightly better ? No!
because it was the last time that he’d hear any-
thing said about a Duke. Were we within three
gasps of eternity we should have felt and said
just the same.
Not that we believe Manning said any-
thing of the sort attributed to him. It is too late
in the day to make us swallow as gospel any more
of the “ last words ” of great men.
The N.S.W. Ministers were content to
present their humble duty to Queen Victoria in
regard to the death of the Duke of Clarence. The
Mayor of Sydney, however, went one better. He,
according to the papers, presented his humblest
duty.
• • »
It was a ’cute thing for the Victorian
Ministry to appoint Sunday as a day of mourning
for the Duke of Clarence. The Munro, from
Aberdeen awa’ or thereaboots, merely remarked ;
“In the meedst o’ life we are in death, but it
inus’na’cut into beesness.”
# # •
In an editorial, Sydney D.T., speaking of
the deceased Duke of Clarence, printed “ he ”
with a capital H. Perhaps it was the sarcastic
comp. The S.M. Herald and Echo were the
only Sydney papers to “ turn ” their column-rules
(he., go into mourning); the Herald turned them
inside its sheet, and the Echo only turned them
amongst its outer advertisements. The latter
fact wants thinking over.
• * #
The S.M. Herald has astounded its
solemn readers by the publication of Mr. Henry
Lucy’s jibing article on the betrothal of the
Duke of Clarence and Princess May in the same
issue in which it wails over the death of the
former.
* * *
The Herald, which in one place allows
Mr. Lucy to speak most contemptuously of the
deceased Duke, becomes in another absolutely
maudlin over his bier. Here is a cablegram from
its “London Correspondent :
The condition of Princess Victoria of Teck causes great;
anxiety to her family. She clings to the body of the
Duke of Clarence, calling, “O, Eddie, Eddie! speak to
me.” The efforts of her relatives to calm her trrief are
unavailing.
This is very pathetic—also very improbable.
There is a good deal of rigid etiquette about the
remains of dead royalties, and even when the
relatives of the deceased become hysterical and
scream over his dust they seldom scream loud
enough for a Herald correspondent to hear them.
However, the clerical journalist loses his sense of
probability through living too much on Jonah
and the whale and little yams like that.
* * #
Mrs. Julian Ashton is considering a sug-
gestion that she should lecture in a Sydney public-
hall, on an early date, upon the marriage question.
Should Mrs. Ashton lecture, she will be sure of
a good audience.
♦ * *
A tremendous swell occupying one of the
very highest positions in another colony got into
a rumpus with a Hobart cabbie who drove him to
Government House. The legal fare to the gate is
one Bishop Smith, but if through the gate to the
big front door it is Is. fid. The enormous per-
sonage with the very red face stopped cabbie at
the gate, paid the shilling, and saved the six-
pence. That beats even Saumarez-S. !
* • »
Raspberries and milk (not cream) have
superseded sardine-sandwiches at entertainments
at Government House, Hobart. Raspberries can
be bought at eighteenpence a bucket, sardines not
under sixpence a tin.
« • •
One of Sydney’s best-known business men
has just died much regretted by every one who
never saw him eat with his knife at the club.
* * *
The new Mayor of Hobart (Reynolds by
name) distinguished himself greatly at the opening
of the Science Congress meeting at Hobart. His
Right Worshipful Nibs was supposed to say just
a few words of welcome to the wise men who had
come from afar—but he was not going to be done
out of his little show. Accordingly he proceeded
to deliver a mayoral address on astronomy, in
which he gave the great men representing that
particular section a hard nut to crack by evolving
a theory that the world was all inside and no out-
side. The audience took the thing pretty good
humouredly and roared with laughter until he
quietly shut up.
* * *
If the Launceston Exhibition Commis-
sioners are let in for a big deficit they have but them.
THE SYDNEY WAITRESS: HER FRIENDLY ATTITUDE.
selves to blame for it. Beyond a few side-shows
of the meanest kind, and a perpetual warfare of
brass bands, they have not put on any of the
thousaml-and-one draws which indirectly make
these big affairs a success. The mere inspection
of exhibits alone soon gets monotonous. The
Commissioners must run extra attractions, or free
drinks, one or the other.
# # *
Old Baron Von Mueller scored off the
Dean of Hobart at the Science Congress at Hobart.
Rev. Dundas, who is a very fightable parson,
wrote to the secretary of Congress, asking by
what right a “dissenting ” parson, named Clarke,
had been told off to preach the science sermon.
Simultaneously with the Dean’s note came another
growl from the R.C. bishop. To smooth matters
down, it was arranged that the three loving
Christian brothers should each have his say. Just
when matters were pretty well settled, the dry,
old Baron looked blearingly over his spectacles
and said, “ Shentlemen!—shentlemen! de matter
hash gone far enougsh, Shentlemen! shentlemen!
we shall get ash dishorderly as der Synod.”
* # #
The return laid upon the table of N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly as to the quantity of un-
claimed gold in Sydney mint completely explodes
the old story of a Western firm having lodged in
the mint a large quantity of gold said to have
been purchased at half its value, and which the
firm was afraid to claim. Very likely it was with
a view to breaking-up the story in question that
the return was called for.
# # #.
When ex-Premier Munro goes to London
as Agent-General for Victoria he will presently
be made a K.0.M.G., for it has become almost a
fixed tradition that every Agent-General must
have a title. Then his exemplary wife will
naturally become Lady Munro. And then when,
the Melbourne papers report that “Lady
Munro” (another Lady Munro, you know) “ was
fined 10s. for being drunk and using violent
language ” a great many ignorant people will get
a totally erroneous idea, and the London papers
will report regretfully that the Agent-General’s
wife has gone on a tear. Munro had better con-
sider this difficulty before he takes his title.
* * #
The eccentric parson who bought the
Model Prison at Port Arthur when it was put up
for sale by the Tasmanian Government, makes a
good thing out of the excursionists who flock to
the “ old settlement ” in the summer, charging a
shilling a head for admission to the interior of
the “relic.” The other day a large party of
visitors from the mainland grudgingly parted
with their money only to find the chief attraction
—the model chapel—closed against them.
* * *
It is not generally known that the present
dilapidated condition of the old prison buildings
at Port Arthur is due to the pilfering propensi-
ties of a former “caretaker,” now dead, who
stripped the roofs of the buildings of the immense
quantity of lead that rendered them water-tight
and smuggled it to Hobart with the aid of con-
federates.
* * *
Dear Bulletin, — In a paragraph, you say :
“ Complaints are again made that well-paid
officers in the N.S.W. Civil Service lend money at
Hebraic interest to their impecunious subor-
dinates. Ministers should see to this.” It is
well known in the Service that it is often an
invidious task for Ministers to interfere. For
instance, in the Telegraph Department one high-
placed functionary lends at the rate of 2s. per
month oh every <£l—that is, 120 per cent. He
prosecutes business with impunity, for he he Ids
two of his few superiors as security for the safety
of his position. It is hard to call on a “boss”
to wriggle from under the thumb of a junior
“ boss ” and give evidence before the Minister. If
a Civil Service magnate can’t move and think and
speak cum dignitate, he sinks into a mere nonen-
tity, and Ministers have always to bear this in
mind. The boss of 20 years’ standing is as
unapproachable as the Czar. —J.M.
* * #
The Sydney typists’ regular charge for an
occasional business letter of average length is one
shilling. For an anonymous or pseudonymous
communication the fee is half-a-guinea. We
mention this for the information of gentlemen
wishing to engage “housekeepers” during their
wives’ absences at summer resorts,

Two hours’ inspection of the N.S.W.
papers on the racks and tables of Sydney School
of Arts reading-room revealed to a Bulletin man,
last week, the publication of 300 columns of dis-
honestly-obtained literary—not news—matter.
Yet Australian litterateurs are expected to keep
out of gaol.
St. Stephen’s Review publishes an article
on Lord Jersey’s “work” in Australia, declaring
that the noble earl has proved acceptable to all
political parties and is absolutely “the best
Governor New South Wales ever had.” God
Save the Queen ! But how about poor Oar-
ington ?

The splendid English steam yacht White
Heather” left Sydney last week en route for
England via Auckland and the Straits of
Magellan. Mr. and Mrs. Leigh, it is remarked,
“ made a host of friends ” during their stay in the
City of the Beautiful Harbor. You may be sure.
Plenty of money, a hospitable disposition, and a
fine yacht, are a good foundation for a one sided
friendship as the world goes. Apropos, any man
with a good whisky-agency and lots- of samples
has lots of friends.

Major So-an’-So, commanding the Blank Permanent
Forces, will shortly marry the daughterof the lion. Blank,
of Victoria. This lady has £4OOO a \ ear in her own right,
but Major So-an’-So does not intend giving up his
military position at present.
“ At present ” is simply beautiful;
* * *
At Melbourne District Court, the other
day, Miss Mears proceeded against Gordon Coutts,
an artist, for the recovery of twenty-eight pun,
five. Miss Mears is a masseuse, who gradually
and scientifically rubbed into Mr. Coutts the
amount stated. The gentleman, for his part, had
delicately suggested that perhaps the other artist
had better stop rubbing, because he was only
running into her debt, but she still kept at it (so
he said), on the understanding that he wouldn’t
be “ pressed.” However, he was pressed, in both a
financial and journalistic sense—evening press-ed,
in fact —and the verdict carried three guineas
costs —

who has now got an inalienable right to the
hardest chair and a seat in the draught at the
Churchill mansion, first rose to fame, if we mis-
take not, nine years ago. At that time a lunatic
named Maclean made an alleged attempt to
"ILL-FATED CLARENCE."
An Heir Presumptive to a throne
Has given up the ghost,
To few of us his face was known,
His name, maybe, to most.
The lying Press in print has wept
And sighed like anything;
We’ve nothing sad to say, except—
He might have been a king.
The Heir Apparent’s eldest son 1
And what was he beside ?
His “ greatness,” with his birth begun,
Was ended as he died.
No special sorrow wrung our hearts
When Clarence passed away,
For men who play more useful parts
Are dying every day,
And working hard on every hand
To earn a pittance mean
Until they reach the Better Land
Which hasn’t got a queen ;
And they, when ’tis their turn to go.
From loving arms are torn.
And grief is in their homes, although
The Nation doesn't mourn.
The Nation Mourns —a canting phrase.
What trouble we, or you,
When princes die ? Let liars praise
The good they didn’t do.
We cannot weep, we do not laugh,
We smoke a pipe and sing
This harmless, honest epitaph—
"He might have been—a king.”
THE BEST WE EVER HAD.
The Baron’s nose is out of joint;
That smart, retired rake
Is not the man to whom we point
As holder of the cake.
Although he always seemed so nice
And tried to play the Rad,
The new one is, to be precise,
The best we ever had.
Preetrade adores him hugely, and
Protection loves him much—
With every creature in the land
The Jarsey is in touch :
He smiles upon the squattah proud
And on the lowly cad,
Till both together cry aloud—
“ The best we ever had !”
He goeth in for Simple joys
With simple, noble Zest,
lie hasn’t sent a batch of boys
To find eternal rest ;
lie sits his Sunday sermon out
And never seemeth sad—
lie really is, without a doubt,
The best we ever had.
Great Carington, thy very name
If half-forgotten now,
Thy laurel wreath of Sydney fame
Has withered on thy brow.
Although we sometimes hear it said
“ The other wasn’t bad,"
The Earl who reigneth in thy stead
Is—best we ever had.
To win renown, we must confess,
Is easy for the “ swell ” ;
A Gov. achieves a great success
By doing nothing—well,
And whilst our Jersey doesn’t make
Us very sick or mad,
He shall be called, and no mistake,
The. best we ever had,
HIS RUBBER.
Dear Gordon, let the maiden have her cash,
And do not snub her ;
Although the love of art has made you rash,
Don't sigh or blubber ;
In days when you were feeling low and ill
You wouldn’t take a plain, plebeian pill,
But said, “ I’ll try, yea, cdute qui Coutts it will,
A quiet rubber.”
She rubbed you, with her left and with her right,
Around the liver ;
Of new and strange sensations of delight
She proved the giver ;
She banished all your little aches and pains,
You dreamed of singing birds and shady lanes,
The while the blood was rushing through your veins
Just like a river.
But whilst the maid was “kneading” with a will,
You noticed, sonny,
She also needed more to pay her bill—
Heigho 1 more money ;
She rubbed in glowing'.health at every pore,
But now there comes the rub that is a bore—
’Tis you who must be rubbing off the score.
Old man, it’s funny.

The latest scheme for Federation
Claims to be for the country’s weal,
While all may prove but botheration,
Which can’t be said of Pemell’s Meal.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap I ween.

murder Queen Victoria, and was instantly jumped
on by many policemen and almost strangled in the
mud. There was no real attempt at murder, for
Maclean only flourished an empty pistol in one
hand and a paper in the other, but the occasion
has always been described as one on which Vic-
toria looked in the eye of death and remained un-
moved. And just at that memorable instant vyben
Maclean had been seized by everybody within
reach, and policemen were walking over him like
ants on an ant-hill, and there was hardly a square
inch of the helpless lunatic that some gigantic
constable wasn’t standing on, Salmon Wilson’s
offspring, then a schoolboy from Eton, burst
through the crowd, and banged the wretched
prisoner on the head with his umbrella.
Next morning the London Telegraph published
an article showing that Wilson, junior, and his
umbrella were powerful evidences of the undying
loyalty of “the kawlinies.”
* * *
The celebrated Sunny Comer silver-mine
appears to have collapsed completely. Twelve
months ago shares were worth 545. apiece; now
they can be bought for sd. A lot of big Sydney
men who bought freely at top prices are in a fair
way to bankruptcy over the collapse.
« « *
All over Australia the coffee-palaces estab-
lished in the cause of “ stout-hearted philan-
thropy ” (and 10 per shent), finding that 10 per
cent, came not, are “turning dog ” on the cause
and are taking out liquor-licenses. The latest to
be turned into a hotel is the Grand Central,
Sydney. We grieve to say it, but the truth must
be told : the ginger-beer hostelry is, wherever you
go in the world, a cheerless hole, full of chilly
people. Next time we are passing the reformed
Grand Central we will look in and take a drink.
The local undertaker at
Oampbelltown (N.S.W.) lately
had a sensational advertisement.
His horses were newly-broken
colts, and after a fortnight’s
spell on rich pasturage they
were hitched up to the hearse
to do “the sober, steadfast,
and demure” gait of funeral
decorum. Soon after the cortege
started they shied and bolted,
dashed past the cemetery at a
hand-gallop, and careered along
the country - road for another
mile ere being pulled up. Every
morning since their owner has
galloped them for ten miles so
as to keep them “ stiff.” The
moral is that any aspirant for
undertaking honours should in-
vest in decrepit ’bus-horses. To
the overworked and knock-
kneed steed the cemetery-road
is the flowery path, and a
horse’s gratitude is all the
gratitude an undertaker can
hope for in this life.
* * #
One of the Melbourne
banks ” has got things down
so fine that the directors and
the auditor now go round a
back lane and enter by the
back door, and the staff does
the same, lest the public should
mistake them for a “ rush,” and
get alarmed about the stability
of the institution. In better
days they all used the front
door and were taken for deposi-
tors crowding in to fix their
money for three years at 10
per cent., but these times are
long since fled.

one wife.
Frock and bonnet items are beginning to
find their way even into the War Cry. In the
course of a two-column scream Mrs. Ballington
Booth urges the hallelujah girls to avoid earrings,
watch-chains, lace, velvet, and silk, not to bang
their hair, to abjure kid gloves, high sleeves,
oxidised buttons and sich, and to heave their high-
heeled shoes at the devil, and, if possible, to land
him in the eye. Then she finishes with this very
practical remark: “I advise our women not to
wear their skirts so short in the front, as we have
to stand on high platforms, and it is hardly
modest. (Mem. here for habitues of the grand-
stand at Randwick races). The dress-skirt should
reach quite down to the feet, below the ankle. If
too long for the march in wet weather, it could
easily be drawn up a little.” Now we know why
the bald-headed convert always makes for the
front seat.

There is hardly a woman in Hobart —
matron or spinster—who is not, at least, secretly
sorry that “ Bing.” Crowther has been arrested,
and many there are who are outspoken in their
regrets.

Somebody suggests that Matthias Larkin’s
library be purchased by the State and presented
to Melbourne University. Better confiscate it,
and present it to the gaol.
A DAY OF SORROW.
THE LONG MAN (loq.): “I say, old fellow, you look a bit down in the mouth.”
THE OTHER ONE: “Well, it’s about time—when the old woman starts in to cut down your oldest boy’s
pants for you.”
In the Church of England, there is likely
to be an ordinance for administering the Com-
munion wine in small cordial glasses, instead of
all drinking from one cup. A clergyman has been
heard to say, “Thank heaven, I always drink
first ” —which recalls the much-kissed court bible.
In some parts of Scotland they hand round one
piece of bread for all the communicants to bite.
* * #
The fact that Lord Rosebery, who has
buried one rich wife, is about to marry
another, reminds people that Queen Victoria once
wanted him to marry Princess Beatrice, an offer
which would have been snapped at by Burdett
Smith or E. L. Zox. L. L. Smith also, when he
was young and had no family ties, would have
been willin’.
* • *
The Surrey Times says that a prominent
reason for Lord Onslow’s resignation of the
Governorship of Maoriland “ is the hopelessly
unsanitary condition of the capital.” An incident
related . by Lord Onslow himself illustrates
another of the vicissitudes of official life in Maori-
land. The Governor’s party was Journeying on
horseback through the South Island, and one
night put up at an inn kept by an old Dutch
woman. In the morning one of the guests com-
plained that he had caught a mouse in his bed.
“ Well, I’m sure I’ve done my best,” was the old
lady’s assuring reply, “for I took seven mice out
of your bed just before you went up."
* * #
A curator’s residence, newly built in the
Brisbane (Q.) Botanic Gardens, has been per-
emptorily pulled down, because its back view
offended the tastes of the residents of Government
House.

Certain provincial N.S.W. Anglicans—and
some other people~are awaiting with eagerness
the steps to be taken by a local church dignitary
in connection with the probate of the will of a
recently-defunct. They are also anxious to know
whether it is an ecclesiastical canon or what which
entitles the “legatee” to take possession before
the death of the “ testator ? ”

Melbourne Age, following up its cable
hysterics with a column of particulars concerning
the Duke of Clarence and all his relations,
managed to crowd an absurd misstatement and a
huge grammatical error into two lines. “ The
Duchess of Teck, though a sister of the Queen, is
considerably younger than her” —said the Age.

In a case of alleged embezzlement heard
in Sydney last week the prosecutor swore that he
paid the accused, who had been in his employ for
11 years as confidential clerk , a salary of £2 per
week.

from Sydney draper’s advt.: —
Ten pair blankets, suitable for charities, sent to any
post or railway station in the colony for 40s.
Then he goes on to advertise another kind of
blanket at 425. each. This sort isn’t suitable for
cold charity.

There have been two or three advertisers
for “ artist’s models ” in the Sydney dailies lately.
A certain Sydney barmaid answered one of these.
The advertiser appeared as an artist of the pro-
curer type —a canvasser for the degradation of

family life in Australia pure and sweet and
beautiful. The old country was more loved in
Australia than at home.” Quite right. “Green
fields afar off;” “familiarity breeds contempt
“castles in Spain,” &e., &c.

The latest Sydney book-fiend story tells of
how a canvasser visited an irate broker, and
talked at him until the broker punched him first
and afterwards threw him down eleven steps.
The broker had scarcely taken his seat again When
the canvasser came back smiling with: “But,
putting all jokes aside, Mr. L , won’t you take
the book, now ?”

According to Minister Lyne, the sale of
the land resumed by Government in Martin-place
facing Sydney Post-office, will more than repay
the cost of resumption and formation. To accom-
plish this it must realise at least .£l2OO per foot f
The auction-sale takes place next month.
That distinguished gourmet, Randolph
Churchill, is so disgusted with the Boers that he
has, it is said, taken upon himself to alter the
Dutch names of localities. Thus Flipt Spruit
becomes Grilled Partridge Creek, and Snikerbosch
Kopje is Reebuck-kidney-on-toast Hill.
“Greenhide Jack,” a hawker, aged 82
years, had his team bolt near Coonabarabran
(N.S.W.), recently, and was thrown out, breaking
“four ribs, one arm and one leg.” If the boy
isn’t more careful, he’ll be hurting himself one of
these fine days.
A Blackwood (Vic.) man the other day
“proved” in an insolvent estate for “Is. 6d.,
three drinks supplied.” As the average dividend
nowadays is 2s. 9d. in the £, this strictly business
man may hope to ultimately receive the sum of
2Jd., and nine-tenths of a farthing, in five pay-
ments extending over 18 months.
It is said that some of the specimens of
Italia korridenta recently imported to work the
North Bananaland plantations are evincing
decidedly amorous sentiments towards the native
beauty of the soil. Tuppence an hour, however,
and that paid in instalments of Heaven knows
what, at periods of Heaven knows when, can’t
possibly go very far in “ yougibitbacea,” and
Guglielmo Juanosa will have to find esoteric
methods of adding to his income.
When the African goes in for style there
is no white swell on earth who can compare with
him. The other day a “cullud pusson” of the
gorgeous variety was dining in a Melbourne hotel.
He had a tall, shiny hat which he hung up on a
peg, huge collar and cuffs, and diamond studs,
and he shone like a garish beetle in the sunlight.
The waiter brought him ham and eggs, but he
only regarded them with a fastidious eye, and
then sent them away. He sent away the chops
too, and when some steak came along he decided
that it wasn’t good enough, and fired it out also.
.Then a big bagman sitting at the other side of the
table suddenly raised his stentorian voice and
spoke: “Waiter, be good enough to get a possum
for this darned blackfellow.” There was peace
after that, and the “cullud gentleman” ate any-
thing that came along.
The mad king of Bavaria has got a new
inspiration; at latest advices he considered him-
self a stork. He has constructed a huge nest in
one of the apartments of his palace of Furstenried,
every stick and straw of which he gathered up out
of the dirt with his mouth and fetched along in
his teeth. And now he sits in his nest most of
the day with feathers stuck in his long, thick hair
and beard, and makes strange clattering sounds
when anybody comes near him. On other
occasions he stands on one leg gazing with a
vacant eye at the air in front of him. At these
times he is very like a stork, which is a stupid
and drunken-looking bird that could pass, on a
back view, for a bald hunchback on one leg hold-
ing on to the tails of a dress-coat. When Otto is
balanced on one foot, with the feathers in his
unkempt head, he is every inch a king—and a
stork, which is much the same thing.
Some years ago the s.s. Wellington was
running between Auckland and Whangaroa (M. L.)
with a large assortment of passengers—chiefly
bagmen. Every second man aboard a Maoriland
steamer is a bagman, and the man in between is
generally a preacher. There was a glaring Hebrew
drummer among the crowd, and when the vessel
got out of Whangaroa he sidled up to the captain
and volunteered to take the wheel as far as the
next port, three hours distant. He said that in
his youth he had been a seaman by profession,
which, considering his nose, seemed an eminently
improbable statement, but the captain accepted
the statement and put him on the stern-wheel.
The other bagmen gathered round to watch
the proceedings. It was a hot day, and pre-
sently the corpulent Israelite at the wheel began
to perspire and breathe heavily. The vessel
evidently wanted a lot of steering ; every minute
the captain called to the suffering bagman to put
her “ hard-a-port,” and the Hebraic one would
whiz the wheel round and stand on the spokes to
steady her, and then before he could wipe the
perspiration from his smoking head there would
be another cry of “ hard-a-starboard,” and the
implement would be rushed over again, and the
steersman would hold it down with his hands and
both feet, and puff despairingly. If the bag-
man’s hat got down over his eyes, or if he wanted
to sneeze, the captain at that very moment
would invariably yell at him|like a tornado, and
make him throw himself on the wheel like a
Hebrew possessed. At first he tried to wear an
air of joint proprietorship in the boat, but pre-
sently that faded away, and he became dejected,
limp, and crushed-looking. Still he hung on for
three weary hours till the next port was in sight,
and then he feebly asked to Jbe relieved “as he
wanted to get his things together.” “ Oh, it’s all
right,” said the captain, blandly. “You can let
go. In fact, the wheel’s out of gear—has been
out of gear all the afternoon. There’s another
man steering in the wheelhouse on the bridge.”
“ What!” “Yes, didn’t you know it ? I thought
you were only amusing yourself, so I kept holler-
ing orders to you to make it look real, you know.”
And then the Hebrew rose up suddenly, and
made remarks. He has been known on the
Maoriland coast as “The Steersman ” ever since.
THE INSTITUTE OF JOURNALISTS.
The other night the drivers of the “ mighty engine” met
To band themselves together as they’ve ne’er been banded
yet,
To formulate a comprehensive, literary club.
With artist and with editor, reporter, “chief” and
“ sub.”
They crowded in an upper-room and smoked a plaguey
lot.
And some of them looked well-to-do, and some—well,
some did not;
The former’d “ chucked ” the Press because the payment
didn’t suit—
And, therefore, they were qualified to join the Institute.
They wore big corporations, and they toyed with choice
cigars,
And looked a lot superior to the men who dealt in pars.—
But all the crowd was merry, and the wit was pretty fair.
And Dow was highly honoured when they put him in the
chair. •
He didn’t strike one forcibly as quite the J. L. Dow
Who’d gone before the City Court—you all remember how;
He yarned and laughed and tippled, and he very nearly
sang;
He joked about his trouble with the Gourlay grabbing
gang. .»
The speeches were explanatory, likewise they were brief,
A fact in which no pressman could see any cause for
grief—
Macdonald spoke a speaklet with his index finger spread,
And all felt “ elevated ” by the things that Tipping said.
Then Robinson—a party who had left the noble Press
For a clerkship up in Parliament—delivered an address,
He said, “ ’Tis forty centuries since I began the game,
But still I’ll move the motion that is standing in my
name."
And Edmund Mitchell seconded ; he wished, he said, to
keep
Acquaintance with some people who were not absorbed in
sheep ;
And Morgan, from the lair of Syme, his acquiescence lent,
And Melvin, of the Telegraph, most kindly did assent.
And then a sub-committee was proposed by Harry Short,
To wrestle with the rules and to bring up a big report,
And when they’d had a toast or two, why Lambie played
the flute—
And thus did eighty scribes inaugurate an Institute.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
■ Supposing AJ.S.W. had beaten South Australia
in the recent cricket-match, South Australia having
beaten Victoria by one innings, and Victoria having
beaten N.S.W. by six wickets, which province would have
been entitled to the premiership ?
# # »
Grace’s ’92 Australian average is, at the time of
writing, 48 for 14 innings, top of the English list. His
159 not out against Victoria, of course, assisted materially.
Phillipson, with only 6 innings, is second with 26. Then
come the three pro.’s, Lohmann (25), Maurice Read (23),
Peel (23), followed by Stoddart, who has only made one
big score.
* # »
The financial divide, amid all hands, over the
last Melbourne cricket-match, England v. Australia, was
the biggest known in cricket annals.
« • •
The Englishmen are pretty careful of their boss
bowlers. Every member of the team, bar M’Gregor, has
been allowed to try his hand in one or other of the
matches against sixteen or twenty-two. Bean and
Maurice Read went on as a “ double change ” during the
second innings of East Melbourne, the other day, and the
Surrey slogger actually made an average of one wicket
for six runs.
* * *
Talk about W. G, Grace’s ill manners after his
team’s defeat in the test cricket match 1 You should just
have seen Lord Sheffield !
* # *
Conynghame should have a show in international
matches, as Queensland, possessing a player of his merit,
should undoubtedly' be represented.
* * *
Ned Gregory’s old horse is, perhaps, the
most knowing quadruped under the sun. In the recent
interprovincial cricket match, when the ground was being
rolled after the first innings, the old horse stopped mid-
way between the wickets, winked at Ned and neighed ;
and Ned will take his oath that neigh was horse-talk for
“ I hope Moulton comes off in the second innings.”
* # #
Prof. Miller wants to fight Joe Goddard. Miller
is apparently tired of life.
* * #
The Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club, like the
London Pelican, has collapsed. Larry Foley’s Olympic
meanwhile flourishes.
* • * *
Last week Charley Dunn, well-known both as a
ped. and pug., though Nature has not given him a good
shape for either job, met Tom Taylor, the blacksmith,
who also teaches the young idea how to shoot the left
and cross with the right, at Sydney Olympic Club- the
old Gaiety Theatre. Dunn, a short man, with extra-
ordinary development of back, chest and shoulders,
adopted a crouching attitude, and should have fallen an
easy prey to Taylor, who stood well up to his man. In
the first round Taylor had all the best of it, but seemed
nervous, another proof of the old theory that, except in
Irish faction fights, red-headed men are “soft.” In the
second round Dunn managed to close and put in some
heavy blows, which completely nonplussed Taylor, who
became dazed. Nothing but the call of time saved him,
and he went out early in the third round.
* * #
A Sydney crowd compared with a Melbourne
ditto is as a raging lion to a tame rabbit. Any row on
the M. C. C. ground is out of the question nowadays.
The people down South are so orderly and highly
governed that if they paid to see a cricket-match and the
cricket didn’t appear they would simply heave a gentle
sigh and slink off, one at a time. Nobody ever saw' a
Melbourne man try to work up a demonstration in a
drizzling rain, or stand up for what he considered his
rights when the wind was blow ing from the North, and in
fine, cool weather he hesitates to assert himself in a
public place for fear of being suddenly removed on a
charge of insulting behaviour. The strange influence of
the Murray in effecting social distinctions must not be
overlooked when the Federation question next crops up.
* # *
Larry Foley will fight Professor Price, of Mel-
bourne, or any other man on earth, with singlesticks, for
as much money as he likes.
* * #
Grace came out to field substitute for Callaway
in the international match, when the Englishmen were
batting for the last time, and at once began to arrange
the home team. The joke was taken in good part, and
reminded one of paterfamilias joining in the cricket-
match between his own kids and those next door, and
telling both what to do. When Callaway re-appeared,
Grace shook him warmly by the hand, and doubled back
to the pavilion, amid general applause. Grace and
Callaway, by the way, were frequently chatting together
on the field during the match.
* # *
The Victorian Cricket Association has decided
that Dr. Barrett’s reason (“ that he went to bed ill on
Tuesday and didn’t get up until the following Monday”)
for not playing in the recent match Victoria v. N.S. Wales,
iS unsatisfactory, and it has been decided not to select
the Doctor any more this year.
Dear Bulletin, —Slavin’s evasion of Jackson’s
challenge indicates to me that he has not forgotten that
little turn-up in Foley’s bar a few years ago. The mob
cry for Slavin, but 1 have all along—unlike other scribes—
held the opinion that should Peter eschew Masonic
junketting and bar-room work he’ll win, not too quickly,
yet easily. Sullivan’s panther-like rushes, were he at his
best, might break down Peter’s guard, but Slavin is too
slow. The history of thel’.R. proves that a quick, straight
left can always overcome a smashing right.—V.
* # *
, Steve O’Donnell, the most skilful of Sydney’s
big boxers, seemingly does not care to see his name in
print, especially in connection with the gloves. The
papers speak of him as an amateur. When he used to
attend Foley’s White Horse some five years ago, there
seemed to be little of the amateur about him.
WITH COLOURED SAILS.
As the National Regatta Committee considered that
coloured sails in yachting would make the pace too fast,
they excluded them from taking part in the National
Regatta, which led to the coloured sails getting up their
own regatta, and inviting the sailing world of Sydney to
enter in their contests the result being that nearly
the whole boat-racing world responded to the call in
sympathy with the coloured club. On Monday the entries
were 83 boats, truly a great, fleet and the pick of Austra-
lian sailing men. The coloured-sail people threaten to
knock the end out of the National Regatta.

Ex-bobby Bally whack, of a glorious little
agricultural town down South, doesn’t depend for
his existence on his neat little police-pension, but
parades around various streets every Monday
morning, for his rents. He was a good policeman
and he prospered. He is still a very good man
and he goes around with the plate at his church
every Sunday. But he recently had a “ bit uv a
differ ” with one of his tenants, a charming young
widow who might yet be changing her late hus-
band’s name, and he evicted her out of one of his
shops, after two months’ hard manoeuvring, she
was such a divil! After that, whenever he used
to carry round the plate on Sundays, and approach
her seat, instead of dropping in the regulation
thrippenny bit, she would start “keening” in a
loud tone —the witch ! —“Ochone! An’ ter think
such a howly, swate-looking, saintly ould man wud
tur-r-n out uv house an’ home a poor, lone widdy, the
loike of me !” He stood it for some Sundays, but
at last had to swop plates with the man in the
other aisle, or gangway, or whatever you call
it. Ballywhack had a mate —or had had one,
we should say, for the like of him would not
speak to the like of this one now —named Hooley,
who had been in the foorce along with him, but
Hooley was quite a different kind of a bird.
While Ballywhack would be always investing in
terraces —that’s the stock saying, you know, but
it was in allotments, in this case—Hooley would
always be stone-broke, with his month’s screw
mortgaged two months beforehand, you under-
stand. He, too, is now retired, but he
is still stone - broke. His principal source
of income is the giving of advice to litigants; he
takes his fees in beer. Ooh ! but he’s the merry
soul! Once, when he was on active “ jooty,” he
went of a Sunday into a pub. to join a dozen or so
friends in a surreptitious delight. Now, the
Inspector had been a mspector of Hooley for a
long time, and happened to “obsarve” him go
into this very house on this occasion. So the
Inspector cunningly waited till he thought the
glasses were all filled, and then he made a rush.
But the boys had heard, him, and every man
pocketed his glass straight away, while Hooley
drew his baton and laid around him right and left,
singing out: “Will yez, ye shpalpeens, disgrace
yer howly religion!” When the Inspector
appeared they all became quiet, and Hooley said :
“It’s all roight, sorr ; ye’re just too }ate, sorr ; the
wretches shtarted a foight over religion, sorr, but
Oi quoitened ’em I”

size 7|.

», woman of California possesses before mar-

This, remarks Gertrude Frances Atherton, con*
sidering that
California is but 45 years old, is a big stride out of bar-
naiism, particularly when one thinks of the mellow-and
rotten—countries left in the rear. What will be the
result when women get the bit fairly between their teeth
is unsafe of prediction. That in time it will alter the very
features on the face of the earth can hardly be doubted.
Hie moral code will be the first to feel the strain, snap,
and then go over to the dust-heap. Women having the
refinement of centuries in their blood will never care to
adopt the utmost license of men, but they will
arrange their lives to please themselves, and if
Jr® 11 ' Pleasures interfere with outworn codes
W1 yawn the latter aside. Matrimony is
f sure to go by the board. Human nature in its
perpetual changes was never intended to endure one
ri a lT n W ie r- Man llas recognised this, and calmly
reciothes his ideal whenever the fancy, suits him. Woman
wui not belong embracing the same privilege. As to the
rights of children—which is always the first argument to
ring up so many will not occur, and the law will attend
to them as soon as they do. Let every child when born,
immediately enjoy the same rights as the California wife
ness does ~ partnershi P in its father’s or mother’s busi-
How Mrs. Atherton would make the titled dames
of Sydney Women’s Suffrage Association sit up !
And just think of the married man of the future
calmly announcing to his wife (or, let us say, his
elective affinity pro. tern.) at breakfast, apropos of
the fact that a bygone and discarded affinity of
his had just added a unit to the world’s popula-
tion : “ Maria, you will be pleased to hear that—
—er—from yesterday another infant partner has
been admitted into my business.” When Mrs.
Atherton comes to'Sydney Lady Jersey will keep
her out of Government House.

DECLARATION.
THOS. MUDIMAN, of 515 George Street, Sydney, in
the colony of New South Wales, do hereby solemnly and
sincerely declare as follows:
Whereas, I suffered very severely from a very
troublesome affection of the Nerves, which affected me so
much that I was quite unable to attend to my business —
that of a Merchant Tailor. I tried several Doctors and
many Remedies, but all of no avail. Whereas, I, as a
last hope obtained some of Herr Rassmussen’s
PURELY HERBAL “ DENMARK UNI-
VERSAL PILLS,” which I hereby solemnly and
sincerely declare COMPLETELY CURED ME within a
few weeks, and although this is now over five years ago, I
have remained well ever since.
The DENMARK UNIVERSAL PILLS also cured
my wife, and I make this solemn declaration, conscientiously
believing the same to be true. i
THOS. MUDIMAN.
Subscribed and declared at Sydney, this 23rd day of
November, 1891, before me,
S. T. Whiddon,
Justice of the Peace.

Eight insolvents faced the music in one
day in Brisbane, Q., last week, six of them in
formd pauperis.
New Thackeray story : “ Sir,” said
Thackeray to a New York Bowery Boy “can 1
get to Houston-street this way?” “Guess yet
kin, sonny ”—spit—“ if yer behave yerself.”
On the Lachlan river, N.S.W., many
patches of land, so low-lying as to be unfit for
settlement, have been set apart and humorously
designated, in departmental provision, “Reserves
for Refuge from Flood.” In many instances
when the river rises a few feet above ordinary
level the said refuge reserves are transformed
into little lakes.
A new process of debts recovery is now in
full swing in Bananaland, and the fierce-eyed
creditor rejoiceth exceedingly. The court, after
due forms have been observed, simply attaches
the debtor’s wages, as it thinks fit. The counter-
move to this is, of course, for a debtor to get-some
trusty friend to secure the first attachment of
wages on a t bogus debt, and then, when the real
creditors came on the scene, the court could not
go behind its own previous order, and the creditor
would have to fall back on his old-time remedy of
going outside to curse *and to glare. This is,
therefore, really an improvement on the old style,
as Jeremy Diddler would get the protection of the
court against his creditors, and its innocent con-
nivance with his cheerful plan of never paying
anybody.
London Daily Chronicle has been run-
ning a silly-season column on “ Marriageable
Women,” and the gifted correspondent, beneath
whose letter occurs the editorial rat-trap-snap,
“this correspondence must now cease,” was the
only one for whom it was worth prolonging. She
—there is internal evidence of the “seek”—pro-
poses that a Marriage Tontine Society should be
formed for the endowment of women on mar-
riage.” All that girls would have to do would be
to pay up entrance-fees and keep up their yearly
subscriptions—a sort of insurance-society, in
which marriage takes the place of death, with the
added luck-chance that the past fees of all mem-
bers who die spinsters, or “ grow out,” are
swallowed up into the general fund further, the
marriages must take place between stated ages,
but that is of no consequence, because a maiden
never gets over 30 years old. This, with the
“ dot ” system, and a marriage bureau as a
relieving side-show, ought to work off the unsale-
able English girls whose representatives have been
weeping in the D.C. Mormonism is said to be
dead, and the Turkish harems are over-womanned.
Dear Bulletin,— Re dispute as to eels
being in Riverina waters, allow me to say what 1
know. About 12 years ago a heavy rain fell at
Deniliquin after a long drought. All the lagoon
beds along the Edwards river were full of dead
gum-leaves and the rain washed these into the
river, which, in a few days, become as black as ink.
The change in the river water evidently didn’t
agree with its inhabitants, for the “yabbys”and
turtles took to the banks en masse while the fish—
Murray cod, perch and bream—being unable to
follow their example quietly turned over and died
in hundreds. Along the sandbanks I saw
thousands of little eels, all about four inches long,
lying dead in heaps mixed up with shrimps and
water insects, for whom likewise the gum leaves
were too much. As I have stated all these eels
were of a uniform size and very small. I never
saw any bigger eels than these in the Edwards
and I think all fish usually found in Riverina
waters find their way into the Edwards, which is
a branch of the Murray. If anything would have
made big eels show themselves—if they existed in
the Edwards—the gum-leaves would have done so,
for even the water-rats deserted the river until it
got clean again.—H. 8., Sydney, Jan. 16, 1892.

RUBBISH BOXES.
The subject may seem an unfragrant one to dis-
course about, but, nevertheless, it opens up ground
for investigation. Dust-boxes are not so utterly
destitute of instructive lessons as one might
suppose. They are largely patronised by the
übiquitous goat and the predatory, homeless
mongrel, who overhaul their varied contents in
the grey dawn. It is an interesting sight to
observe a pensive ruminant enjoying a hearty
breakfast off an empty jam-tin, or the remains of
a wornout bonnet, fished up from the depths of a
dust-box, and then trotting merrily homeward to
supply some family with the result of its repast in
the shape of milk.
Dust-boxes have a language of their own. An
inspection of their contents, for instance, will tell
the tale of frugality or extravagance on the part
of the house-keeper whose back fence they adorn.
Observant landlords are taught by them, and
permit no arrears of rent to accumulate where the
rubbish-bins display too many glass monuments,
recognised by the initiated as “dead marines.”
The thousands of dozens of bottles which once
contained fluids of various brands, and that find
their way into the dust-boxes of rich and poor
alike, might be worked up as a subject for an
interesting temperance lecture. More interesting
still would it be to note the large number of care-
fully drained phials bearing the well-known St.
Jacobs Oil label that find a similar destination.
An enormous percentage of Australian homes are
never without it, and in the remainder it is either
borrowed from a neighbour when occasion requires
or the patient has to suffer on. Everybody who
has tried it, or who knows anyone else that has,
declares that as an external remedy for pain St.
Jacobs Oil is pre-eminently the most efficacious in
the world.

A POPULAR MISTAKE.
Many people have an idea that the Waterbury,
because a cheap watch, is therefore cheaply made.
A visit to the company’s factory, at Waterbury,
Conn., would decidedly alter this impression._ On
seeing the extent and variety of the machinery
used, and the neatness, order, and organisation
there exhibited, you begin to realise that making
a cheap Watch (and a good one) is not by any
means the off-hand affair you had thought it was.
As you pass thtough department after department
and observe the workihg of wonderful automatic
machines, which make parts so fine you can
scarcely see them, and yet so accurate that they
can be" taken at random and fitted together to
form a perfect watch ; as yon note the handling
of the parts by dainty and skilful fingers, the
delicacv of the process, and the thoroughness and
care as to every detail, you find it difficult to
imagine that they are not making hpndred-dollar
watches ; and you then become convinced that
the Waterbury is cheap, not because poorly made,
but because unique and simple in construction.
