Malchook’s Doom-A Story of the
Nicholson River.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was Malchook who told the beginning of this
story, and Malchook was supposed to be the
biggest liar in the Gulf of Carpentaria, which is
equivalent to saying that he was the biggest liar
in the world. However, it was on record that he
told the truth sometimes —when he was in a blue
funk, for instance, and on this occasion his state
of funk was a dazzling purple—blue was no name
We were camped on the Nicholson for the wet
season. The cattle had been turned out and we
had pretty hard work to keep them together, for,
after the rain set in and the country got boggy,
the niggers commenced playing up and we had to
keep going. It was raining cats and dogs that
night and we were all huddled together round the
fire under a bit of a bark lean-to which we had
put up. Malchook was away —his horses were
absent that morning and he had been away all
day after them. It was about eight o clock when
we heard him coming; he had found his horses
and was driving them right up to the camp. Then,
instead of hpbbling them, he got a bridle and a
halter, caught them and tied them up to a tree.
Some of the fellows sang out to him to know
what he was doing, but he took no notice, and,
after turning out the horse he had been riding,
came up to the fire and told Reeve (the boss) that
he wanted a word with him. Reeve got up, and
the two went over to his tent. Presently Mal-
chook came out, went over to where his duds were
under the tarpaulin that had been rigged up over
the rations, and commenced to roll them up.
Reeve came back to the fire.
“What’s up ? ” asked Thomas, Reeve’s cousin.
“ Only that fellow wants to leave to-night,
straight away, so I gave him his cheque and told
him to slide as soon as he liked ; he’s no great
loss anyway.”
“ What does he want to leave for ?”
“ Says the camp is doomed and he is going to
put as many miles as possible between himself
and us before our fate overtakes us.”
There was a general laugh, and just then Mal-
chook came out with his swag and commenced to
saddle up in the pouring rain. There was a good
moon, nearly full, although of course it was not
visible.
The fellows commenced chaffing him, for he
was not a favourite; too all-round a liar. He
stood it without a word until he was ready to
mount, then he got on his horse and, turning
round, said: “Laugh away; this time to-morrow
I ’ll have the laugh of you ; this camp is doomed !
He stuck the spurs in his horse and disappeared
swish, swish, swish, through the bog down the
bank of the river, and we heard him swearing at
his pack-horse as he crossed the sand.
There was much laughter and wonderment at
what had sent Malchook “off his chump,” but
eccentricity was common in those days, from
various causes, and presently we all turned in.
I was sleeping under the tarpaulin where the
rations were stored, and about two o’clock in the
morning I suddenly awoke. It was brilliant
moonlight, the wind had changed and the rain
ceased, only a little scud was flitting across the
sky, giving the moon that strange appearance that
everybody must occasionally have noticed—as
though she was travelling at express rate through
an archipelago of cloy diets. Some impulse made
me get out from under the mosquito-net and go to
the opening at the end of the tarpaulin and look
Everything was very still and quiet; all the
horses were camped, for not a bell could be heard,
and I stood for some time aimlessly listening and
looking at the glistening pools of water lying on
the flat between our camp and the bank when
suddenly I distinctly heard a human voice in the
bed of the river. I waited for a moment to make
sure, then I got my Martini and a, couple of
cartridges and sneaked towards the river. Last
full-moon the niggers had nearly clubbed the cook
in his mosquito-nets when he was sleeping outside
the tent one night; this time, I thought, it would
be a case of the biters bit. 9
About a hundred yards from the camp! stopped
and listened ; the voice was much nearer, it was a
white man’s, it was Malchook’s, and he was kick-
ing his knoeked-up horse along and dragging the
pack-horse after him. I waited behind a tree
until he was close up, and then I stepped out. 1
was only in my shirt, with the carbine in my
ha,1 ‘Great God ! ” he cried, with a kind of choke,
“ he’s here again ! ”
“What the devil is up with you?” 1 said;
“ why didn’t you stop away when you went ? Got
bushed, I suppose, and the horses brought you
back ?”
He’sat on his horse and panted for a few
minutes without speaking; then he said: inat
infernal old nigger wouldn’t let me go. He hunted
me back. I’ve got to share your fate, so let’s get
it over.” , . , ... T
He jabbed his heels in his horse s ribs, but i
stopped him. “ Don’t wake the camp up, I said.
“ What nigger do you mean ?”
“ The nigger that Jacky the Span and i roasted
in the spinifex. He’s headed me back every road
I’ve tried, and I give it up. Let me turn the
h irses out, and try and get a wink of sleep.”
Jacky the Span was an old blackguard of a
Mexican who had been knocked on the head about
six months before. Everybody said he richly
deserved it, and everybody was right.
“ When were you up here with Jacky Span . l
asked. , . ~
“ About two years ago; tire time Bratten was
killed ; but let me turn out the horses and 111 tell
you all about it.” , . ,
We went quietly back to camp, let the tired
horses go, and then Malchook laid do.wn on
his blankets alongside of me. The tarpaulin
was rigged some distance from the other tents,
and the boys were done-up and sleeping sound, so
nobody awoke. This is what Malchook told me -
Two years before, he and the old Mexican had
come up to join Bratten in mustering some horses
that had got away from the lower part of the
river and were supposed to be knocking about
below the first gorge. Like most half-breeds,
Jacky the Span (short for Spaniard) was a
most inhuman brute towards the natives when-
ever he got a chance, and Malchook,
a blowhard and a bully, was naturally of the
same cowardly disposition —most liars are. One
day they spotted an old man and a young
gin at the foot of a spinifex ridge that runs in on
the Upper Nicholson. I knew the place real
old man spinifex that would go through a leather
legging. They rounded the old black up on the
top of the ridge, but missed the gin, and Jacky
Span said he would make the man find her or
ho should suffer, and Malchook, in order to keep
up his reputation as a flash man and a real 010
Gu llhand, aided and abetted him.
i suppose the poor devil was too frightened
to understand what they really wanted, but, any-
how, all the half-caste devilry, which is the worst
devilry in the world, was roused in the Mexican*
and Malchook followed suit. ,
They selected a bank of _ old man spinifex, and
rolled the naked nigger i n it for sport. Now,
spinifex is beastly poisonous stuff ; get your shins
well pricked, and it is worse than any amount oi
mosquito-bites for irritating you and making you
itch. Horses will not face it after a day or two
in really bad country, and if you run your hand
down their skins you will soon see the reason why.
Every little prick festers, and their legs are
covered with tiny boils and ulcers after a few days
in bad spinifex. The niggers always burn it
ahead of them before they travel through it. Uut
in the real Never-Never they have regular tracks
that they keep burnt down.
By the time they had rolled this nigger in
the spinifex for some minutes, he must have been
in a raging hell of torment; and he knew no
more what they wanted with him than he did at
the start. Then, according to Malchook, Jacky
rolled him into a big bank of dry stuff T they had
tied his feet together—and set tire to it. bpmi-
fex is rare stuff to burn, it is full of turpentine,
and burns with a fierce heat and a black smoke,
so the old nigger was well roasted ; and when it
burnt out they rolled him into another and set
that alight. A gust of wind sprung up and
started the whole ridge ablaze, and the gin, who
had been hidden close by, watching them, sprang
out and ran for it, and Jacky Span picked up the
old man’s club and took after her. He was away
about half-an-hour; meantime the old fellow died,
groaning awfully, and Malchook began to feel as
if he had better have let things alone.
Presently, Jacky Span came back with the
club in his hand—big two-handed clubs they use
out on the Nicholson-and showed Malchook
some blood and hair on it, and laughed like a
devil. No need to repeat here all he said.
Now, if Malchook had there and then blown a
government road through the brute, there might
have been some chance of repentance left for him,
but he didn’t. He sniggered and let Jacky Span
tell him all about it, and camped with him lor
weeks afterwards. Jacky Span was killed, as i
said before, and Malchook assured me, in a sweat-
ing blue funk when he spoke, that just at dark
he had met his horses coming back, with the old
roasted nigger behind driving them. He went
on to say that this thing had followed him right
up to the river and shrieked at him that he would
die in the camp. Then he went on to tell that
Avhen he tried to get away from the camp that
night the old nigger had met him at every point
of the compass, so at last he gave it up and came
'" Now I knew that there had been an importa-
tion of brandy lately into the Gulf country,
generally known as the “possum brand, each
bottle of which was calculated to make a man see
more devils than any six bottles of any other
brand. It was very popular, for it would eat holes
in a saddle-cloth, so I concluded that Malchook
had got hold of some of it, for one of the fellows had
returned from Burketown that day. 1 his would
account for the ghost of the blackfellow, but the
rest of the yarn about Jacky Span I knew to be
true so I told Malchook to clear out and sleep
somewhere else-I wouldn’t have him under the
same tarpaulin with me. He begged and prayed
to be allowed to remain, but I told him I would
wake the camp and tell everything if he didn t go,
so he went, sobbing bitterly. I explained to him
that the best thing he could do was to shoot him-
self • that a man who could follow the lead ot a
miserable half-caste out of pure flashness was too
contemptible to live, but he didn’t appreciate my
kindness, and slouched away to a bit of a sand-
hump about 150 yards from the camp, and I saw
him throw his blankets down and then lie down
on them. I got into my bunk again and went
fast asleep in two minutes.
Reeve woke me up. It was broad daylight.
“The niggers were here last night, ” he said.
“ Did you hear any thing ?”
“ No,” I replied; “ but Malchook came back ; i
saw him.” , , , , , ,
“Yes. They knocked him on the head—bashed
his skull in. He was sleeping out under that tree.
I suppose he was ashamed to wake us up.”
“ Nobody hear anything ?” I asked.
“ Not a sound. There are the traces of about
six niggers coming out of the river towards the
camp, and they must have stumbled right on top
of Malchook. Poor devil! Polished him off and
cleared out. The camp was doomed for him,
after all.” , ,
I concluded to say nothing, beyond having seen
Malchook come back and speaking to him. borne-
times I wonder whether I was not responsible for
his death by hunting him away from the camp,
but I always console myself with the reflection
that he only got what he deserved.
BINGHI.
Mining at Sudesfc (New Guinea) and St.
Aignan, the neighbouring island, still lingers on.
The old hands make wages, but they have to
work hard for it, and when supplies run short
have to subsist on sago, like the natives. Un
Sudest (Tagula) most of the work is fossicking
and dish-washing. At St. Aignan (Misima) they
so in for sluicing. There are about 40 Europeans
on each island. At Sudest some of the natives
manage to obtain a little gold on their own
account and trade it with the Europeans, but at
St. Aignan the formation is against them. Some
reefs have been opened out and some stone sent
to Queensland, but the reefs are very thin.

Murphy’s Selection.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When Isaac Dodd, owner of Doolabin station,
heard that 040 acres, the pick of the run, had
been taken up on the creek, he felt inclined to go
out and defile the grave of the legislator who in-
troduced the first Land Act.
“ Good heavens ! it’s the best piece of grass-
land for miles round, and has a splendid frontage
to Doolabin Creek, and after giving me the sole
u_-e of it for nearly 14 years it is an accursed piece
of Government injustice to allow it to be selected.”
There was not much logic in Dodd’s argument •
but like many of his class he hated the very name
of selector, and in any case he never was logical.
Since the first Land Act became law it had cost
him much money to buy out the selectors who
took up land on the run. Also, it had cost
much dummying and hard swearing to secure
oyer 70,000 acres of the finest country in the
district, and this new invasion made him especially
mad. , J
piece of ground taken up by a man named
Murphy was situated within a few miles of the
homestead, and in such a position as to cut off
Dodd from his best paddocks. Lor a good many
years would-be selectors were led to believe that
it was freehold, but one Friday morning this ruse
came to an end, and James Murphy quietly
walked into the local land office and secured the
choice block. Thoroughly conversant with the
land laws, Dodd did his utmost to upset the
application, but for the nonce met his match.
. “Look here. old man,” ho said, finding’ that
bullying didn t work, “ you’ll never make a living
on tins piace. Chuck it up, and X will give vou a
trifle to go and select better country elsewhere.”
“No fear,” said Murphy, as lie dug a hole’for
the first post. “I’ve seen that sort of thino-
worked too often; I’m going to put in the residen-
tial clause, and then if you like to come to me I
may take five pounds an acre for it.”
This was a part of one of the many conversa-
tions the two men had, but they always ter-
minated in the same way. Also, with each
succeeding conversation Murphy’s language grew
stronger. One day, when the squatter had been
pushing matters pretty warmly, he stopped work-
ing and issued an ultimatum: “For Heaven’s
sake, take your carcase from here. Aren’t you
satisfied with what you’ve got, without wishing to
own all the earth? Look at the men you’ve
bought out, and tell me how much better off are
they for selling their homes to the like of you ’
Now, look here, Mr, Dodd, if you value a sound
skin, takß my advice and keep off this selection,
tor 111 put the dogs on you the next time you
come through those slip-rails.”
Dodd took the advice, and though it went
against his grain to see huts go up, paddocks
tenced off, and the timber rung, he came no more.
But all sorts of unaccountable troubles descended
upon Murphy. The boundary-rider had strict
orders to impound any of the selector's stock
found on the run. More than this, his gates were
constantly found open, his slip-rails down, and his
fences damaged. Also, the inspector of condi-
tional purchases, who always dined well at
Dodd s, paid him more attention than he did to
all the other selectors put together.
But the five years were nearly up, and then
Murphy s troubles would, to a certain extent be
oyer. One day, to his great surprise, Dodd made
his appearance at the hut.
“Well, you’ve come again,” said Murohy, as
he leaned over a sheep he was skinning.
Y\;s, but as a friend this time. Look here,
your term will be up in less than six months,
won t it ?”
‘‘Yes.”
Well, when you get your certificate I’ll give
you <42 10s. an acre for the land, and cash on the
nad. It s more than it is worth, but I want it for
a purpose.”
No, I didn’t put nearly five years’ hard graft
on it to sell it to you for a mere song. I told you
some time ago that if you liked to give me 45 an
acre for it you could have it, but I’ve altered my
mind. I don’t feel inclined to sell it, for I’ve got
a family to keep, so good day.”
Dodd didn’t take the hint, but continued to
stand near.
, Well,” he said, as he watched him take the
sain and fling it over the fence, “you might think
better of it if I call to-morrow.”
You needn’t call, for I’m going to the town.
My wife and youngsters are sick, and the boys are
away shearing.”
“ Yery well, you’re a fool.”
“ Perhaps.”
I say,” continued Dodd, “ you kill a good few
sheep. ”
“ Supposing I do, I pay for them, and I have
men as well as children to feed.”
“ Do your own killing, I suppose ? ”
“Yes.”
“How often?” continued Dodd, in quite a
friendly way.
About two a week now that stripping is on,
and I have extra, men,” gruffly replied Murphy,
making his way inside, and leaving the squatter
nothing else to do but mount and depart, which he
did with a face that betokened mischief.
Murphy was absent for three days, and returned
to find the me&t-Ccislc pretty low. In the evening l
he killed a sheep and had just slung the body to a
beam when Dodd appeared, accompanied by a
mounted trooper.
“ Well,” said Murphy, “ back on the old
errand ?”
Yes. I want to know if all those skins on
the fence are off your own sheep ?”
‘‘Yes. Why do you ask ?”
“ Vou kill nothing but those branded by you ?”
said Dodd, taking no notice of Murphy’s ques-
tion.
Yes. But what the devil are you driving at?”
I have a warrant to search your premises. I
have lost a number of sheep, and my suspicion is
directed to you. Constable Brown, let us look at
these skins.”
Go and look, and be d dto you,” said
Murphy, continuing his job.
I hey walked to the fence, and the first two
skins lifted from the top rail were plainly marked
with Dodd’s tar-brand.
“ Murphy, are these skins off your sheep ?”
“ Yes, I told you before.”
Dodd stepped aside, and picked up the head of
a sheep lately severed from some carcase. “ And
this is your breed ?”
course >” replied the unsuspecting Murphy.
I hen 1 charge you with sheep-stealing. These
skins are branded with ray brand, and this,”
pointing to the head, “has my ear-mark.”
Needless to go closely into the matter. Murphy
was arrested and tried in his own town. His
friends rallied round him to a man, and the best
counsel was secured. He pleaded not guilty. An
able barrister pointed out how hostile the squatter
had always been, and how easy it was for anyone to
carry the skins and the head of a sheep on to the
premises during the absence of the accused, as the
men slept in a shed in the wheat-paddock. The
very acts of the prisoner pointed to his inno-
cence, and it was the duty of the jury to
acquit him. A number of witnesses were called
to testify to the good character he had always
borne; but in spite of all this the jury, a mixed
one, faded to agree. He was tried again at
another town some 30 miles off, with a similar
result, and a third time at yet another town,
where a squatter’s jury brought him in guilty,
without the slightest hesitation, and he was sen-
tenced to five years in a country gaol, the'first
nine months in solitary confinement.
He has just finished his term, and has been wel-
comed back by sundry friends, who are still posi-
tive that he was innocent of the charge brought
against him. Further, there are men who openly
say that the whole thing was “worked up.” At
any rate he came out to find that the adminis-
trators of the land laws of N.S. Wales deemed
that he had failed to comply with the residential
clause, that his selection was classed as for-
feited. It is now part of the Doolabin run.
This sketch is not an imaginary one for the
facts related are part of the liistory of the
N.S.W. land laws, the names alone being fictitious.

Byron was an English pianoforte-tuner,
who had been in Australia only about four
months. Like the ordinary Englishman, he
thought that he could run rings round the Aus-
tralians in everything. It was his second trip
round the N.S.W. Western districts, and he
carried a breech-loader with him in the buggy.
He boasted considerably of the number of
opossums he was shooting daily, and said he was
going to cure their skins and send two rugs to
England. The “boys” at one of the western
hotels decided to take the conceit out of Byron.
One day at dinner-time one of them asked another
how many white opossums he had shot the pre-
vious day. Byron was interested at once. He
made numerous enquiries, and was crammed with
full particulars as to the locale of the white
opossums aforesaid. Next day, acting on the
advice of the boys, he sallied forth in the broiling
sun about 11 a.m. The ring-barked paddock
which he had been informed was teeming with
these curiosities about noon every day, was three
miles from town. To this paddock, and all over
its 1280 acres, and then back to town, Byron man-
fully walked. Needless to say he brought back
no opossums of any kind. He left that even-
ing after taking tea alone in a private parlour.
Next time he visited the town he stayed at
another hotel. The boys have not yet ascertained
whether he sent “ home ” the rugs. When ques-
tioned on the subject Byron is as silent as the
Oneen’a af.atiifi.
What Mattel's It ?
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
What matters it if, by-and-bv,
Our Aryan race should waneand die.
And Mongol with dark Ethiop vie
To rule the wide world, by-and-by ?
Infinity of Time hath Seen
Changes as strange as this, I ween—
What matter’d it ?
What matters it if we rebel
Against life’s misery, and sell
Thu weary years, we know so well,
For Death’s exchange ? They’re ours to sell'
And when we lie in endless sleep,
Should one or none in memory weep
What matters it ?
What matters it, lives good or ill,
For self or kind? we but fulfil
Our task as doth the water-mill :
The current flows—it can’t be still,
Should summer dry the flowing stream,
Our work is done, was it a dream ?
What matters it ?
What matters it? the end comes fast,
The sea is wild, the night-cloud cast,
A comrade’s aid in vain is asked :
Sauvc quipeut! is best at last.
Together sink the true and base,
A careless Fate, death marks each face—
What matters it?
What matters it ? What matters all
In life—if life be end of all ?
Our loves and longings fade and fall ;
And sadly glide beyond recall
The years which should be brightest, best,
Then asks our heart, sick with unrest—
“ What matters it ? ”
Cook town.
A STRAYAWAY.
Two tons of human hair, the largest mass
ever gathered together in the world’s history, were
given, by 50,000 women of Japan, to make the
necessary ropes wherewith to hoist into their
places the beams of a temple erected to a god
who never heard or answered a prayer, the cost of
the temple being .£1,400,000. They cut off and
gave that which was their crowning feature of
beauty, at the simple request of the priests of
their god.
The Maoriland “native-interpreters”—
mostly Englishmen—who often have learnt the
language from book-sources rather than from the
Maoris, often make ludicrous mistakes, especially
in cases where native words, somewhat similar in
sound, have an altogether different origin and
meaning from each other, and where the distin-
guishing accentuations are so vague that the
meaning can only be gathered from the context.
For example: “ Tai po ” signifies “the night
tide,” and “ taipo ”is “the Devil.” Also,
“ tai epa ” is “the morning tide,” and “ taihepa”
“a fence.” An old, dignified native is giving his
evidence, in which he describes an attack upon a
hostile “ pah.” He has stated that his son, who
has been fighting at his side, received his death-
wound from a bullet fired from the “ pah.” As
his son fell, the old man turned to him, crying:
“ Depart, my son, on the evening tide (tai po) ; I
will follow on the morning tide (tai epa).” A
poetic simile, .by which he expressed his in-
tention of following his son to another world
after meeting his death in a morning attack on
the stronghold. The inexperienced interpreter
knocked all the poetry out of the sentence when
he translated it thus ; “ Go, my son, to the devil;
T will follow on the fence.”

Otherside.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Somewhere in the mystic future, on the road to Paradise,
There’s a very pleasant country that I’ve dreamed of once
or twice,
It has inland towns, and cities by the ocean’s rocky shelves,
But the people of the country differ somewhat from our-
selves ;
It is many leagues beyond us, and they call it Otherside,
And there is among its people more Humanity than Pride.
Now, a social system never was complete, without a flaw,
And among the Othersiders there is love and gold and
war.
But if one is fairly beaten he can turnupon the track,
For, in such a case there isn’t any shame in going back ;
And a broken-hearted mortal never thinks of suicide,
For he finds amongst his brothers more Humanity than
Pride.
And the lords of that creation never scoff at s.tuple
things—
Never scorn the lad who’s tethered to his mother's apron-
strings,
He will speak of “ home ” and “ mother ” without shame
when he’s inclined.
Yet the blow he strikes in battle mostly leaves a mark
behind.
They are brave against invasion—they can die in Other-
side
Tho’ there is among the people more Humanity than
Pride.
Poets sing in simple language that a child might under-
stand,
Yet their songs are sung for ages by the elders of the land,
And the people know that Freedom never shall be wanting
guards,
For the foremost in the vanguard waves the banner of the
Bards. "»
Oh ! the poets march together, and at homo in peace
abide.
For there is amongst the people more Humanity than
Pride.
And when I am very weary, ’neath a load of “ worldly
care,”
There are times when I've a longing Just to hump my
bluey there ;
But alone I could not reach it - for the track is barred to
one—
I must take the nations with me—all mankind must go, or
none—
And we’d trample one another on the way to Otherside,
For I find among my brothers less Humanity than Pride.
Sydney, December, ’9l.
HENRY LAWSON.
IT RECALLS THE BLIZZARD.
In March, 1888, the great blizzard in America
almost extinguished the city of New York. The
like of it was never before seen. The snow com-
pletely stopped all local traffic. Not a horse or a
wheel could move. All the telegraph lines lead-
ing into the city were prostrated, and for two or
three days the people of Boston and New York
communicated with each other by way of London
by means of the Atlantic cables. Thus messages
intended for three hundred miles distant were
sent six thousand miles, crossing the ocean twice.
An incident which recalls this experience to the
writer’s mind happened a short time ago here in
England. Suppose we let the gentleman in-
terested tell his own story. He says : “ I have
suffered more or less from indigestion and
dyspepsia all my life. I had a bad taste in the
mouth, pain after eating, a poor appetite, and
sour stomach. My tongue was coated, and my
mouth constantly tilled with a watery fluid. No
matter what I ate, however light, it disagreed
with me and gave me pain. I had fulness of the
chest, and pain at my side, with a miserable, low,
dull feeling. From time to time I consulted a
doctor who gave me medicines, but they did very
little good. The doctor said that the coating of
my stomach was disordered, and the mucous
m •mbrane was inflamed. In 1877 I received a
pamphlet from New York telling of a medicine
called Mother Seigel's Curative Syrup, and of the
extraordinary cures it had effected, so I procured
a supply, and after taking the first 1 felt better,
and by tire time I had taken four bottles m re, I
was completely cured, and have been in good
health, as regards the indigestion, ever since.
Having studied the practice of herbs for many
years, I was in the habit of treating people for
erysipelas and other complaints, and was fre-
quently consulted by people in the district, so
after my remarkable cure, I was so impressed
with - the merits of Seigel’s Syrup, that I procured
a large supply of the medicine, arid' recommended
it to all who were suffering, and people came from
far and near for it. I may mention that on a
S ‘.vday my house was beseiged by miner's from
C >al Pit Heath, and others from a distance. On
every hand I heard nothing but the loudest praise
o‘ it and of the cures it effected, and the fame of
this medicine was spread throughout the West of
England with no other advertising than one party
telbng another of the benefit they had derived
from this wonderful medicine. I wish every one
to know of this, and if by publishing this state-
ment it will help others who may be suffering as I
was, it will afford me pleasure.”
The letter from which the foregoing is an
extract is signed Moses Godwin, Old Sodbury
(Sudbury), Glos., and is dated April 9th, 1891.
He is a farmer.
The reader will notice that while the head-
quarters for the sale of Mother Seigel’s Syrup is
universally known to be in London, by a strange
chance Mr. Godwin’s first information concerning
it came from America, three thousand miles away,
which recalls the incident of the American blizzard
above narrated, and also shows that the fame and
usefulness of this medicine extend to all civilised
countries.
Mr. Benjamin Edgerton, grocer and provision
dealer, Plat Lane, Whixall, Whitchurch, Salop,
says: “While living with Mr. Roberts, Fens
Woods Farm, I first began to feel a dull, heav>
weight at my side, and noticed a bad taste in the
mouth with foul stomach and uncomfortable fee -
ing after eating. I had no appetite, and when I
sat down to the table I could riot touch the food.
I had a good deal of pain and noise in the head,
and could not sleep for it. 1 was not fit for heavy
work, and could only do light jobs about the
farm. After tutting a hedge I would go quite
faint, and had to sit down, and felt so much
depressed I could have cried. Having always
been such a strong man I took it badly to be
reduced to such a weak state. I took all kinds of
physic and saw a doctor, but his medicine only
eased me for a bit, and then I was worse than
before. I went on in this way for over a year,
when a servant tlfat came to live at Mr. Roberts
told mo of a medicine called Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. She had heard a gentleman talking about
it in the railway carriage, and he praised it so
much that I thought I would try it. After I had
taken two bottles my food did me good, and I
gained strength, and by persevering with the
syrup I soon got as strong as ever, and have never
ailed anything since.”
The late Earl Lytton died of “ heart-
failure.” In other words, he used to smoke 100
cigarettes daily.

The population of Italy is about that of
England, but during 1888 no less than 820,000
persons died in Italy and only 520,000 in
England.
At a meeting of the creditors of an un-
fortunate insolvent some time ago, John Harris,
the lately deceased millionaire of Shane’s Park,
N.S.W., voluntarily and without consideration,
cancelled a debt of <£5000.
A London writer remarks that Parnell
was a born commander of armed men, and it was
the irony of Fate that he should have become the
leader of a party in Parliament. Then he goes
on to say : —
The stormy scenes of 1878-9, when obst ruction in the
House of Commons was at ils height, will never fade
from my memory. Parnell’s blanched face as he stood up
before the storm, like a rock on which the surf was beat-
ing, comes vividly before me as I write. At that time no
effectual means of meeting obstruction had been devised.
The House had only one resource ; it could not make a man
sit down, but it could drown his voice with clamour, and
this it did with Parnell. The obstructive speech was met
w ith a volume of “ Ow, ow, ow's !” which rose and fell with
a curious regularity. For hours at a stretch Parnell
would be patiently engaged in wearing out the powers of
endurance of his adversaries, edging in what he had to
say word by word and syllable by syllable. Such a word
as “ imperturbable ” it used to take him five minutes to get
over. Let me give a sample of a method of warfare
which, under the rules devised to meet it, has long since
passed into the domain of history, and will never be seen
again. Parnell (loq.): As I (Gw, ow, ow !)—as I look
—(Ow, ow, ow !)—look upon- (Ow, ow, ow !)-the face—
(Ow, ow, ow !)—of the right hon- (Ow, ow, ow !>r-honour
— (Ow, ow, ow !)—honourable—(Ow, ow, ow !)—gent—(Ow,
ow, ow !) —gentle—(Ow, ow, ow !)- man —(Ow, ow, ow !)—
oppos— —(Ow, ow, ow !)-oppos — (Ow, ow, ovv!)-op
(Ow, ow, ow !)-opposite—(Ow, ow, ow!) And so
on Occasionally the temper of the man would give way,
and then, with a look of hate, he would hiss something
through his teeth without waiting for the customary lull.
The great desert of Sahara is gradually
being reclaimed under the sedulous care of the
irrigationists. Nearly fourteen thousand artesian
wells have been sunk in French Algeria. About
twelve million acres have been reclaimed, and the
once barren, death-generating sand prairie is
becoming productive of the grape and palm.
Fifty years ago or so the English yacht
Wanderer touched at Sydney and reported that
the owner, Mr. Boyd, had landed upon Guadal-
canal Solomon Islands, and gone hunting, after
which he was never seen again. An American
paper now reports that the island natives aver
that Boyd was shot by men of his own party who
landed with him.
The N.S. W. licensed surveyors have a real
grievance in the matter of the now Ministerial
regulation anent the issue of annual license foes,
and for once wo partly agree with the Sydney
daily press. Surveyors’ permits can hardly be rated
as trade licenses ; they are certificates of efficiency
merely.
Queensland mangoes have of late been
sent to Sydney in large quantities. The sale has
been somewhat checked by the high prices asked,
but still all supplies up to date have found buyers.
The mango, in the opinion of those who have
sampled most of the world’s fruits, is the most
delicious of all. Mungo-eating is said to be an
“acquired taste,” but most people somehow
manage to acquire it in two minutes after inter-
viewing their first mango ; and once a liking for
the fruit (a thoroughly wholesome one) has been
contracted, the appetite becomes a perfect passion.
Mangoes are not a dessert-fruit; they cannot be
eaten gracefully. The best way to eat them is to
take off most of your clothes and sit in a paddock
alone alongside a case of mangoes, a bath-towel,
and a big bucket of water. As in Burmah the
King and Court stop all business and go into
complete privacy when the dorian is ripe, so
Australia, should the Bananalanders continue to
enlarge their tropical-fruit plantations, may yet
adjourn for a general holiday when the mango
becomes yellow up North,
A Wellington (M.L.) family, named
Edwards, went out to a picnic the other day, and
by all accounts enjoyed themselves thoroughly.
They got home about dusk, and knocked at the
door, but the expected servant-girl didn’t come to
open it. When they got tired of waiting they
looked through the keyhole in the hope of seeing
somebody coming along the hall, and amid the
uncertain shadows inside one member of the party
thought she saw two feet that seemed to be rest-
ing on the door-handle inside. It was such a
surprising place for feet that the picnic party
unanimously burst through the window to in-
vesligate and found the,servant girl in a partially
dressed state suspended from a rope at the back
of the door. She had let The fire go out, and
hadn't even laid the table before hanging herself.
Also the gas was turned off, and the corpse had
mislaid the matches, arid just at that moment it
began to rain, and what with the darkness and
the slushy sounds outside, and the darkness and
the dead servant inside,)it was a solemn conclusion
to a picnic. That family will go on no more
picnics for a long time.
